The Denver Post


October 23, 2008 Thursday
FINAL EDITION


Election cash leaves Colo.


BYLINE: Karen E. Crummy The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-01


LENGTH: 746 words


Despite assertions that it was not cutting back on resources in the state, John McCain's presidential campaign has drastically slashed television advertising at Colorado's big three stations.

At the same time, national Republicans have canceled $600,000 worth of ads supporting incumbent GOP Rep. Marilyn Musgrave's re-election bid. And the campaign arm of Senate Democrats pulled out of the Mark Udall-Bob Schaffer race, where recent polls show Udall, a Democrat, with a wide lead. Colorado's days as a battleground state for the Nov. 4 election may be waning.

McCain, who trails Barack Obama by an average of 5 percentage points in Colorado polls, this week bought a total of $305,550 worth of ads at KUSA-Channel 9, KCNC-Channel 4 and KMGH-Channel 7, according to records. That is a 46 percent decrease from the week before and a 56 percent slide from two weeks ago.

For the final eight days of the campaign season, McCain has purchased a little more than $50,000 of ad time at Channel 4 and $67,000 at Channel 9, which is watched by 40 percent of the market. McCain had not purchased ad time for those days at Channel 7 as of Wednesday.

By comparison, Obama has purchased more than $500,000 worth of advertising at Channel 4 and Channel 9 for the final week. Add on the $287,700 he has laid out for ads at Channel 7, and it's nearly seven times what McCain is spending.

Campaigns often adjust their advertising buys based on priorities and can subtract or add, as long as the stations have slots available.

"What this means is that the McCain campaign has sparse resources and is beginning to write places off," said Jennifer Duffy, managing editor of the nonpartisan Cook Political Report. "Colorado polling is consistently looking bad for McCain. So it doesn't surprise me that they are putting their resources elsewhere."

McCain opted to receive public financing, which totaled $84 million post-convention. Obama opted out and raised $150 million for just the month of September.

Members of both the local and national McCain campaign have repeatedly denied they would - or did - shift or cut back on resources, including ads, in Colorado. When asked about the drop at the three stations Wednesday, Tom Kise, spokesman for the campaign, refused to answer the question and instead discussed the ads themselves.

"We are not pulling ads and will be on the air in Colorado through Election Day. While we won't be discussing specifics of any buy, we reserve the right, like all campaigns do, including the Obama camp, to make strategic moves on a daily basis with our paid media efforts," Kise said.

Regardless of the ad situation, McCain himself will be in Colorado on Friday, campaigning in Denver and Durango. Obama will be in Denver on Sunday.

Musgrave airtime pulled

McCain's ads were bought either by his campaign or his victory committee, which includes money from the Republican National Committee.

On its own, the RNC has targeted Colorado, as well Florida, Indiana, North Carolina, Missouri, Ohio, Pennsylvania and Virginia, for advertising. This week the RNC is spending just under $500,000 in support of McCain, but the campaigns are prohibited from coordinating with each other.

In Colorado's closest-watched congressional race, the National Republican Congressional Committee pulled about $600,000 worth of airtime set aside to support Musgrave's race for the 4th Congressional District. Musgrave is running against Democrat Betsy Markey in a district that includes Fort Collins and Greeley. While there have been no recent public polls on the race, political crystal-ball readers put Markey ahead..

The Musgrave campaign reported $798,000 on hand as of Sept. 30, while the Markey campaign reported $382,000.

DSCC confident of Udall race

The Democratic Senatorial Campaign Committee will stop running ads targeting Colorado's open Senate seat, state Democratic sources said Wednesday. The withdrawal signals that the group believes Udall is comfortably ahead of Schaffer in the race and can shift its money to other competitive races.

Despite the vote of confidence by national Democrats, Udall's campaign said it wasn't happy with the retreat.

"We think that outside GOP groups will see this as an opportunity to launch more mean-spirited attack ads against Mark," Udall spokesman Tara Trujillo said.

Dick Wadhams, head of Schaffer's campaign, said the move was typical of Democrats being "cocky" and "arrogant."

Staff writers Mike Riley and Jessica Fender contributed to this report.

The Denver Post


October 22, 2008 Wednesday
FINAL EDITION


McCain "fighting to win" Colorado GOP ad blitz, visit by candidate slated


BYLINE: Karen E. Crummy The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-04


LENGTH: 335 words


If John McCain and the GOP are planning to cede Colorado to Democrat Barack Obama, they sure have a funny way of showing it.

Not only is the Republican National Committee's independent expenditure arm targeting the state with big ad buys, but McCain - who will be flooding the airwaves here through Election Day, his campaign said - is slated to make three campaign stops in Colorado Friday.

McCain's visit comes on the heels of three appearances in the state earlier this week by his running mate, Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin. Her husband, Todd, visited Colorado on Tuesday.

"John McCain is fighting to win Colorado. Just look at where the campaign is sending the candidates," said Christian Ferry, McCain's deputy campaign manager. "We have a very strong campaign there, and reports that we aren't fighting to win is hogwash."

Citing two anonymous McCain "strategists and advisers," CNN reported Monday that the McCain campaign saw its chances of winning Colorado (and Iowa and New Mexico) as "bleak" and was looking for ways to win the election without Colorado.

But the RNC independent arm, which is not bound by spending limits, has put Colorado on its target list, spokesman Brad Todd said.

The group is spending more than $18 million for advertising in seven states - Colorado, Missouri, Indiana, Virginia, North Carolina, Ohio and Pennsylvania - to run through Election Day.

"How can anyone say we're going anywhere? We're spending a boatload of money in Colorado," said Frank Donatelli, the RNC's deputy chairman.

Obama currently leads McCain in Colorado by an average of 5 percentage points, according to recent polls.

or kcrummy@denverpost.com

The Denver Post


October 20, 2008 Monday
FINAL EDITION


Intense courting of Colorado The state's unaffiliated ranks draw candidates in to make personal pleas to voters considered critical.


BYLINE: Karen E. Crummy The Denver Post


SECTION: DENVER & THE WEST; Pg. A-01


LENGTH: 839 words


With 15 days left in the presidential race, Republican John McCain is struggling to hold on to Colorado and a handful of critical red states that President Bush took four years ago.

The Arizona senator, saddled with an unpopular president and a tumbling economy, finds himself in the precarious position of trailing Democrat Barack Obama by an average of 6 percentage points in a state that has backed only one Democratic presidential candidate in the past 40 years. His running mate, Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, is campaigning today in Colorado Springs, Loveland and Grand Junction.

The political climate, along with a number of other factors, including Obama's steady performances in three debates and McCain's choice of conservative Palin, is dragging McCain down in Colorado, some political observers say, primarily because of the state's moderate, unaffiliated voters, who now narrowly edge out registered Republicans.

"The race is still too close to call, especially because Obama is considered a liberal and Colorado voters don't easily digest liberals," said Tom Cronin, a political-science professor at Colorado College who has studied the Interior West's voting behavior for four decades. "But many of these independent voters are looking for a definitive change. And McCain hasn't helped himself very much in that area."

Additionally, the GOP is suffering from a huge voter-registration loss. The 180,000-voter edge Republicans had over Democrats four years ago has whittled down to fewer than 50,000, according to registration numbers through September. Whether new or unaffiliated voters - who rarely lean toward a candidate en masse - turn out for Obama remains to be seen. But the Illinois senator has 51 offices in the state devoted to get-out-the-vote efforts, whereas McCain has 12.

Rick Davis, McCain's campaign manager, acknowledges that the "mass financial global collapse" and the "most unpopular president since Nixon" have hamstrung his candidate's move forward. But, he said, there are still a significant number of undecided voters in Colorado and other states, and they, along with independents, are swayed not by Obama's "big government" message but by McCain's traditional federalist approach to finding solutions without government.

"We are very steadily chipping away at undecided voters with our message," Davis said. "Obama has tried to declare the election's over. But John McCain closes extremely well."

As many as 5 percent of Colorado voters are undecided, according to recent polls, and while Obama has a significant lead among unaffiliated voters, a Rasmussen Reports poll two weeks ago found that a third of Colorado's unaffiliated voters said they could change their minds.

Davis pointed out that McCain is fighting hard for Colorado. In addition to Palin's stops today, McCain is scheduled to be in Colorado on Friday, although no further details have been released.

Obama, however, isn't letting up either. His running mate, Sen. Joe Biden, is slated to campaign here Tuesday and Wednesday. And Obama will make another visit to the state before the election, said Jim Messina, his chief of staff.

Obama's strategy in the state, which started during the primary, has specifically targeted new and unaffiliated voters through repeated door-to-door visits, mail and calls, as well as having a highly visible grassroots operation that has saturated the state.

"The best defense is a good offense," Messina said. "We've always said that if people could meet or talk to (Obama), we would win. Our field organization is an extension of that."

In recent days, McCain has been forced to defend red states - such as Colorado, Virginia, North Carolina, Indiana and Ohio - where Obama has been making inroads. At the same time, a host of polls indicate McCain faces serious challenges in picking off any blue states that went for Democrat John Kerry four years ago.

And even as McCain battles to hold on to traditional GOP states, Obama - who has a massive financial advantage because he opted out of public financing - is actively trying to pull them away, even increasing voter mobilization and ad buys in West Virginia.

Florida, which once appeared squarely in McCain's column, has become a hard-fought contest, primarily because of the economy, said Dan Smith, a University of Florida political-science professor. McCain is forced to spend dwindling time and money to try to capture the state's 27 electoral votes.

"McCain cannot lose Florida," Smith said. "If he does, it's over."

Karen E. Crummy: 303-954-1594

or kcrummy@denverpost.com

Daily Camera (Boulder, Colorado)


October 19, 2008 Sunday
First Edition


SECTION: BROOMFIELD


LENGTH: 4097 words


Republicans to blame for ills

I have been criticized several times for my "leftist" letters. I have found that an open-door Democrat in a heavily Republican community will constantly be harassed and insulted. Sometimes they act more like gang members rather that real Americans. It's no wonder we have so many closet Democrats in Broomfield. But I still believe in the First Amendment. I will be heard, or read.

Recently Tom J. Lloyd and others have said I had a discredited source in the IQ of Bush and Clinton. However, none have ever verified what their real IQs really were. Were they afraid my statistics were really close or accurate?

I follow sources I get from Time, Newsweek, The Denver Post and, yes, the Internet. How do we know that Snopes isn't a Republican operated Web site any more than some people claim the Denver Post and Time are leftist? Or Rush Limbaugh is far right? In an earlier letter I quoted statistics from Newsweek and Time magazine. I was criticized by claiming I had liberal socialistic tendencies by merely quoting from those magazines the percentage of Americans who were complaining about lack of health care and day care.

Tom J. Lloyd stated I should think for myself. You're right Tom. I usually write what I think myself, but no one wants to believe it until they see it later for real or in the news. I predicted Bush was lying about Iraq and he was ruining the country four years ago, but your people considered what I said "a dogmatic leftist vitriolic writing." Here is something you can't disagree with, leftist or real, mainly because of this Republican administration America is in the tank.

Americans were lied to about Iraq. More than 4,170 American soldiers have died, more than 30,000 seriously wounded, more than 125,000 Iraqis dead (check "snopes"). And we now have, if we include the $700 billion payout, more than $11 trillion in national debt. That is more than $36,184 for each person in the United States, including children, illegals, etc. etc. We are mistrusted and disliked by most countries in South America, the Middle East and Europe. We do have a few allies, but they don't really trust us and pretend so they can get our protection and aid.

Our education system is about 14th in the world. We have a 30 percent high school dropout rate. We have 47 million without health insurance, we have more than 27 million Americans living below the poverty level. We have more than 5,000 gun deaths a year. Please, Please, look it up and write us all another letter quoting the more real recent statistics from Snopes.

We had the best country in the whole world, and it is being ruined by the current Republican administration who has had the tiller for the last eight years.

Do we want more of the same with McCain?

Leonard Muniz,

Broomfield

Amendment 48

is a bad idea

A s my girlfriend and I made the final, steep ascent over the boulders to the summit of Bear Peak she

turned to me and asked, "So when I eat a plate of scrambled eggs, am I technically eating a chicken?"

"Right," we both laughed, enjoying the panoramic views spread out in front of us.

We were, of course, talking about the ridiculous Amendment 48 on November's ballot seeking to grant "personhood" to a zygote. The amendment defines person to include any human being from the moment of fertilization. Yes. That's right: Equal protection under the law for an embryo.

Like my hiking friend, I consider myself pro-life and pro-choice. But this proposed amendment is too over-the-top. If passed, we stand to see birth control pills and IUDs, for example, outlawed because they can prevent a fertilized egg from implanting in the uterus. This "Personhood Amendment" as it is called, is a spectacular act of complicity designed to confuse and muddle so many issues. The unintended consequences of this hasty and ill-thought proposal are staggering.

If this amendment is approved, it would apply the definition of "person" to sections of the Colorado constitution regarding equality of justice, due process of law and inalienable rights to a fetus. Fertilization will be the new starting point of equal rights and privileges for everyone from the unborn to senior citizens. This arcane amendment would ultimately end up costing taxpayers thousands of dollars, clogging up courts, impacting laws ranging from when property and inheritance rights are recognized to birth control and health care. Think about it.

In anticipation of possible approval of this measure, consider the deep effects that

would hamper in-vitro fertilization, stem cell research and effectively ban birth control. Imagine being a rape victim and being denied access to emergency, day-after contraceptives.

Kristi Burton, the home-schooled 20-year-old sponsor of the amendment, suggests playing word games with women's health. She founded what she calls Colorado for Equal Rights, and created the first constitutional amendment of its kind in the country and succeeded in gathering enough signatures to get it on the November ballot. And she deserves credit. But I know Colorado voters are much brighter than she suspects and clearly will not support this.

Amendment 48 is wrong not only because it is too extreme. Its basic premise that fertilized eggs, embryos and fetuses are human persons deserving all of the legal rights and moral standing as an infant is wrong. Even my most conservative colleagues see the trouble with that premise. So on our way back down the mountain, my friend turned to me and asked another question. "If Amendment 48 passes, how many passports does a pregnant woman carry when she travels?"

Hmmmmm. We laughed again. "Maybe three if she's carrying twins."

Bette Erickson,

Broomfield City Councilwoman,

Ward 3

Easy fixes for

energy efficiency

H igh energy prices over the past few years have caused millions of Americans to evaluate their homes energy-efficiency. Now is a great time to make just a few simple changes so you can significantly reduce your energy usage and costs. Gov. Ritter proclaimed October

"Energy Awareness Month," and has provided some easy energy savings tips for Coloradans.

You don't have to invest a lot of money to save money. Try these easy no-cost energy savings tips: First, set your thermostat to 68 degrees and save around 10 percent a year on your heating. Also, set your water heater to 120 degrees. Next, make the most of Mother Nature's sunlight by opening window coverings on south-facing windows to warm your home. If you have large windows that don't receive direct sun, keep the drapes closed. Finally, when washing clothes, use cold water; 85 to 90 percent of the energy used to wash your clothes is used to heat the water.

The following low-cost tips will give you an incredible return on your small investment: Be sure to replace your furnace or heat pump filter regularly. Next, install water-efficient showerheads and faucets. Low-flow shower heads also are a worthwhile investment that will reduce the amount of hot water you use, and hence the energy needed to heat it. Also, switch to compact fluorescent light (CFL) bulbs. These bulbs provide as much light as regular incandescent bulbs while using just one-fourth the energy. Finally, save up to 10 percent on your energy bill by weather-stripping doors and sealing windows.

Although it may take some extra money and time on your part, the following tips will go a big way in making your home energy efficient: A large amount of heat is lost from un-insulated homes, so be sure to properly insulate your home. Insulating ceilings, walls, attics, floors, crawl spaces and basements to recommended standards can reduce heating and cooling costs by 5 to 25

Whitcomb earns

vets' endorsement

T his Nov. 4, the voters of Senate District 23 are presented with a unique opportunity to elect competent, professional representation in Joe Whitcomb.

Joe Whitcomb is a brave American who has twice answered his country's call to serve -- in the United States Army as an Ranger who parachuted into Panama to assist in liberating that country from the narcoterrorist regime of Manuel Noriega, and then after 9/11, when he re-enlisted to once again defend our freedoms and way of life.

Captain Rick Duncan, USMC

Jesus would not

endorse Obama

I n the Oct. 5 paper, the faith section columnists were asked the question, who would Jesus vote for and why? I found this topic inappropriate and offensive; it is not something I expect to see in my hometown paper.

I appreciated that the Rev. Charles Reeder's column suggested that after you have humbly submitted yourself to God you could then question which candidate's heart is most conformed to his. Mr. Rudland, on the other hand, stated that Jesus was a community organizer just like Obama and that alone shows Obama's intent to bring justice back into this world. I find this comparison ludicrous. The community that Jesus organized was his apostles and disciples who formed his church. The same church that today, as it has for more than 2,000 years, values human life. According to the U.S. Conference of Catholic Bishops, "Since its beginnings, Christianity has maintained a firm and clear teaching on the sacredness of human life. Jesus Christ emphasized this in his teaching and ministry."

In his recent book "Render unto Caesar," Archbishop Charles J. Chaput writes "My friends often ask me if Catholics in genuinely good conscience can vote for 'pro-choice' candidates. The answer is: I couldn't. Supporting a 'right' to choose abortion simply masks and evades what abortion is: the deliberate killing of innocent life. I know of nothing that can morally offset that kind of evil."

I find it very hard to believe Jesus would vote for a man that has a 100 percent NARAL rating and has stated that the first thing he would do as president is sign the Freedom of Choice Act, in a speech to abortion advocates worried about the increase of pro-life

legislation at the state level. The Freedom of Choice Act (FOCA) is legislation Obama has co-sponsored along with 18 other senators that would annihilate every single state law limiting or regulating abortion, including the federal ban on partial birth abortion. The Born Alive Infants Protection Act (BAIPA) was signed into federal law in 2002. The bill protects infants who are born alive after botched abortions. Prior to 2002, Obama voted against the state version three times. Sen. Obama is also quoted as saying "Look, I got two daughters -- 9 years old and 6 years old," he said. "I am going to teach them first about values and morals, but if they make a mistake, I don't want them punished with a baby. I don't want them punished with an STD at age 16, so it doesn't make sense to not give them information."

Volunteer with

elders, enrich life

I have been working at Broomfield Skilled Nursing and Rehabilitation Center for a little over three months now. I entered this field through volunteer work. When I became employed at the Broomfield Skilled, I began to realize I was going to be paid to work for people who were living in the "old age" stage of life. I started to go home at night and ponder what it really meant to be an elder and why I found this line of work so rewarding.

I felt enriched when I became a volunteer for elders because I was saturated in wisdom, experience and a deep sense of history. I did not grow up surrounded with very much family. I was short of grandparents and other older relatives who could share their experience, strength and hope of how to live life. I do not feel short of family now. I have a deep respect for the people I work for and feel compelled to inspire others to discover the reward of working with elders.

The thing about aging is all

of us will do it. The only difference between the elders I volunteered for and me is they have more time and experience in life than I do. After I began volunteering I asked myself what it meant to be in "old age." What is the value of aging? How do I want to live when and if I ever move to a nursing home later on in my life? What do I hope the people who work around and for me will feel about old age when I become an elder? For me, I do not leave my experience of working with elders feeling drained and empty. I leave the experience feeling filled up.

I am 30 years old. I have been through a divorce, the death of my father and the experience of having and raising a child for five years. Some would say that was a lot for 30 years. Every day I talk and interact with the elders at Broomfield Skilled Nursing, I am humbled by all that time has showed them about life. Life still has much to show me. Life can be hard. Life can be scary. Life can be rewarding beyond all measure. How do I know this? I work with elders. They teach me that if I keep going, whether it is through a world war or a disappointment life has handed me, I will make it. I am honored to be a part of these elder's lives.

How do you feel about elders in our community?

Broomfield Skilled is always looking for enthusiastic people to volunteer. There are many areas to become a part of. We need your talents and your hearts. We are all a part of this community together. Come volunteer and feel a part of something great! Help enrich your life by enriching the lives of our elders.

For information about Broomfield Skilled Nursing Center visit the Web site at www.broomfieldnursing

center.com.

Kathryn Getman,

Broomfield

Look beyond

corporate blame

I t is understandable that disillusioned voters increasingly seek to blame big business for hijacking the Republican Party. But to stop there in our quest for reform, we risk missing the perspective necessary to rescue our political system, not to mention the Republican Party in the process.

We must learn the lessons from the marketing strategy that has pervaded that party at all levels. When Nixon employed the "Southern Strategy" to entice southern Democrats to change parties it was the beginning of an end-justifies-the-means morality that has since taken over the party. The emphasis has increasingly been on strategy rather than substance. Ginrich took this to a new level, and Rove far beyond.

It is now about power and maintaining power. Morality and values have become nothing more than words; words used outside the context of ethics to achieve whatever the marketing objective is. The Republican strategists have been selling a "brand." They approach politics as if they were selling Nike or Disney. When they sell the Republican brand effectively they don't have to get into discussing "product" features. Note: In the case of politics, the actual products are the policies and programs having to do with health care, the economy, foreign relations, etc. Along the way the Republicans have also prospered from the strategy of discrediting the competing brand (Democrats) with negative labeling. Once on the

defensive, the Democrats have found it difficult to get the public to focus on policy differences and on real issues.

Now that the economy has tanked, however, the strategy of brand-selling is not working so well. And, with voters weary of the war, it is as if nominee McCain can't find a product to sell. Hence, Obama's strategy of focusing on Democratic values and voter needs, and away from personal attacks, is working. Now that Obama has our attention, it looks as if he will have our votes. But once elected it is vital that he provide us with policies that work and which meet our needs. If he is successful it can serve as a model for traditional Republicans to take back their Party and for America to return to a two-party system free from the current divisiveness.

This is an important opportunity -- we can't afford to squander it.

Tom Parsons,

Publicity chairman,

Broomfield Democrats

 Obama policies

patent Marxism

R obin Hood's gambit: Rob the rich, pay the poor. Sen. Obama told an Ohio man on Oct. 13, the self-employed plumber will pay higher taxes because he plans to "spread the wealth around." Except the senator doesn't call it income redistribution.

Euphemisms like "change" or "fairness" play better. So it's only fair the rich pay for the poor. It'll be "good for everybody." Really?

First, I am not calling Sen. Obama a Marxist. A CPA with long experience, I disclaim any intention here of an ad hominem attack. His policies are patent Marxism, and a fair target. Unfortunately, some readers will misunderstand. To those who don't, please listen up.

Marxism defined: "From each according to their means, to each, according to their need," Karl Marx. The Federal Tax Code structures progressive tax rates; as bracket income increases, the rate of tax increases. The lowest individual rate is 15 percent; the highest, 35 percent. In 2006, the latest federal data available, tax filers with above $50,000 in taxable income paid 90.3 percent of all personal income taxes; 56.9 percent, (below $50,000), paid 9.7 percent, 3.8 percent, (above $200,0000), paid 50.6 percent. One-third of tax filers paid no tax. Without question, our already socialist tax structure causes high earners to pay a disproportionate

share of taxes. Sen. Obama asserts this is not fair because it is not enough.

His published tax plan promises to lower income taxes on 95 percent of taxpayers through refundable targeted credits. So though one-third pay no tax, they'll get a refund. He will raise capital gains taxes by one-third, taxes on ordinary income above $250,000 by one-twelfth and corporate taxes by half. CCH analysis estimates the cost of his tax policies at $160 billion per year, net.

Perilously, this plan assumes falsely that GDP will not drop below its current level. U.S. history has demonstrated that raising tax rates on business and capital inhibits GDP and job growth. Small tax increases aside, Sen. Obama's extreme hikes will contract GDP, because the confiscatory tax rates on business will force millions of layoffs and thousands of small businesses into bankruptcy, massively increasing unemployment and welfare. Where is real concern for the poor in such a plan? Despite new tax revenues, the deficit will be severely exacerbated. Do not be deceived. Either Sen. Obama misunderstands taxation, or he's counting on your misunderstanding.

Sen. Obama's change to definitive socialism will exact an unacceptable human cost by crushing middle and lower income Americans, reduced to dependency on the state. Of course, the classless state is the Marxist means to pipe-dream utopia. America has demonstrated just the opposite --working people rising through expanding economic opportunities. The American Dream's realized promise floats all boats. Marxism drains the pond and calls it fairness.

Therefore, the fundamental question in considering Sen. Obama for president is: Are you ready for America to take the Marxist path? Maybe. But when you vote, and I hope you will, make an informed choice.

D. Connor,

Broomfield

The Denver Post


October 19, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


Lessons from the crisis Some see '99 law as enabler, demand greater accountability


BYLINE: Miles Moffeit The Denver Post


SECTION: BUSINESS; Pg. K-01


LENGTH: 1609 words


As the federal government takes a dramatic step toward taking ownership in U.S. banks, financial experts say lawmakers are avoiding the grittiest questions.

Will Congress demand greater accountability of financial firms' leaders? And does a 1999 deregulation law that heaped more responsibility on banks to police their own risks provide key lessons?

The Bush administration and Congress passed caps on senior executives' pay as part of the recent bailout package, but a September Securities and Exchange Commission audit has inspired some experts to call for wider corporate-governance controls, such as requiring banks' directors to assess risk.

"The single biggest overlooked issue in the current crisis is the failure of corporate governance," said J. Robert Brown, professor of securities law at the University of Denver, who believes that Congress and states should hold members of bank boards, as well as top officers, to higher regulatory standards.

Some current and former lawmakers told The Denver Post that they, too, believe the government should look at expanding corporate accountability to help prevent the economy from later sliding into a sinkhole.

The bipartisan 1999 Financial Services Modernization Act has received its share of criticism for enabling the current conditions.

The act, aggressively lobbied for by the finance industry, removed banking restrictions that limited speculation and prevented merging commercial and investment firms and insurance operations.

It helped spawn broader and riskier investments without strict federal oversight.

Some lawmakers and economists refuse to place blame squarely on FSMA, also known as the Gramm-

Leach-Bliley Act because its lead author was Sen. Phil Gramm, R-Texas. They say the law, opposed only by Rep. Joel Hefley among Colorado's delegation at the time (Rep. Scott McInnis missed the vote), has prevented banks from totally collapsing.

For example, even though financial-

services giant Bear Stearns became larded up with bad mortgage investments, FSMA paved the way for JP Morgan to acquire it earlier this year.

"Good adjustments"

"That bill is actually an example of how we made good adjustments to the industry," said Steve Wymer, spokesman for Republican Sen. Wayne Allard, who voted for FSMA, as did both Coloradans trying to succeed him in the Senate, Democratic Rep. Mark Udall and former Republican Rep. Bob Schaffer. "It has allowed firms to consolidate and not go under."

Still, other financial experts and market regulators say the law is the tipping point in a wider decades-long deregulation push that led to dangerous pursuits, such as high concentrations of mortgage-backed securities investments that crippled Bear Stearns.

"The failure of (FSMA) to give regulatory authority over investment bank holding companies to any agency of government was, based on the experience of the last several months, a costly mistake," testified SEC Chairman Christopher Cox in a hearing on Capitol Hill last month.

Cox and the Bush administration also have been assailed for decisions relaxing capital rules on investment firms, enabling them to pile up debt and risk.

But deregulation's roots run deep and are partly shaped by campaign-finance largess. In the early 1980s, Congress removed rules overseeing the savings-and-loan industry. Then through the 1990s and earlier this decade, lawmakers and federal agencies eased restrictions on creditors and securities firms.

Both FSMA, which gutted the Glass-Steagal Act limiting bank speculation, and the 2000 Commodity Futures Modernization Act, deregulating derivatives, followed a major lobbying effort by the finance industry.

The industry pumped at least $86 million in campaign contributions to lawmakers between 1997 and November 1999, according to the Center for Responsive Politics. FSMA, arguably the most far-reaching deregulatory measure, passed Congress with wide bipartisan support, 452-65.

Lawmakers who favored FSMA accepted twice as much industry money at the time as those who opposed it, according to the Center for Responsive Politics.

Voluntary program

Those experts who see wisdom in adding new corporate-governance rules point to a September SEC internal audit. Some large investment banks such as Bear Stearns came under some SEC scrutiny through a voluntary program called CSE. The SEC's inspector general, however, said in the audit that many of the program's guidelines had little teeth.

For example, SEC examiners knew of excessive concentrations in the firm's portfolio of mortgage-backed securities - products now targeted for purchase in the federal government's $700 billion bailout plan - without taking action, including alerting board members.

"Although (the team of examiners) was aware, prior to Bear Stearns becoming a CSE firm, that Bear Stearns' concentration of mortgage securities was increasing for several years and was beyond its internal limits. ... (The team) did not make any efforts to limit" the company's exposure, according to the internal review.

And examiners "took no actions to assess Board of Directors' and senior officials' tolerance for risk," the sentence reads.

In light of what happened to Bear Stearns and others, some congressional representatives sought corporate-

governance requirements as a condition of the bailout, including caps on executive compensation and a say for shareholders in pay decisions.

Only a provision curbing executive compensation and banning golden parachutes has been preserved thus far. That clause also has been criticized for loopholes that will still allow company bosses to rake in large sums.

Brown, the University of Denver expert, said the September SEC report raises important questions about how engaged the Bear Stearns board directors were and why Congress doesn't demand more accountability for such directors industrywide, in addition to more oversight powers for federal agencies.

States such as Delaware, where more than half of the Fortune 500 companies are incorporated, have failed to hold corporate board members accountable for risk, Brown said, requiring only "a process for receiving information."

Congress could pick up where it left off with Sarbanes-Oxley, passed in the wake of Enron and other corporate debacles, that largely mandated enhanced accounting oversight, he said.

Said Brown, "In this bill, Congress could have said something along the lines of 'It's the responsibility of the board of directors to assess risk and specifically require that they obtain information about risk.' Right now they don't have that obligation."

Ed Paisley, vice president of the Washington-based Center for American Progress think tank, said some congressional leaders' promises to add protections to the current bailout measure, in addition to ongoing economic troubles, mean "everything including corporate-governance controls will be wide open for consideration."

Such controls weren't even debated at the time of FSMA's passage in 1999, given the confidence in the market to put a check on itself, said Paisley, who monitored that process.

"What they should have done and did not do was set in place regulatory structures for a brand new world of finance," he said.

Other economists are less optimistic that more regulatory efforts to strengthen corporate governance will work. Jonathan Macey of Cornell University refuses to blame FSMA: "This crisis is not a matter of abusing regulations. This is greed."

Top campaign contributor

New reform efforts could hit stiff resistance from the financial sector.

If its track record of campaign contributions is any indication, the industry still commands steady influence. And for donations since 1989, the sector - covering finance, insurance and real estate businesses - ranks as the top industry contributor in federal elections, steering more than $2 billion to candidates and political parties, Center for Responsive Politics research shows.

Udall said through a spokeswoman that he doesn't regret voting for FSMA, because it was important to help banks maintain a competitive position in the "global" marketplace.

"That doesn't mean the regulators did enough to enforce the other rules or that we shouldn't take another look at stricter regulation," said Heather Fox, a Udall spokeswoman. "And I expect the next Congress to do so" regardless of lobbying pressures.

Former Rep. Schaffer, now running against Udall for Allard's seat, sees FSMA as only "marginally related to the meltdown."

But given the size of the bailout, he favors legislative efforts to explore corporate-governance standards. "I would like to see further efforts with respect to transparency from an investor's standpoint."

Miles Moffeit: 303-954-1415

or mmoffeit@denverpost.com Campaign giving

by financial industry

Top congressional recipients of campaign contributions from the financial-services industry:

Hillary Rodham Clinton $31 million

Barack Obama $27.9 million

John McCain $26.5 million

John Kerry $19 million

Colorado lawmakers

Amounts given to the state's current congressional delegation:

Sen. Ken Salazar $1.65 million

Sen. Wayne Allard $1.44 million

Rep. Mark Udall $1.26 million

Rep. Marilyn Musgrave $780,317

Rep. Ed Perlmutter $587,179

Rep. Diana DeGette $500,176

Rep. Tom Tancredo $451,256

Rep. John Salazar $391,050

Rep. Doug Lamborn $125,720

Former Congressman Bob Schaffer, who was in Congress half as long as Udall, received at least $47,350 from the industry between 1997 and 2001, records show. Former Rep. Joel Hefley, the only member of the state's delegation at the time to vote against FSMA, received $56,700 from the sector between 1999 and 2006.

Source: Center for Responsive Politics research covering donations between 1989 and 2008

First Edition


World Series schedule could hurt Fox


BYLINE: Tom Hoffarth Los Angeles Daily News


SECTION: C:DAILY CAMERA; Pg. 04


LENGTH: 740 words


R eading between the lines and through the thick brush, attempting to connect a few dots from the more interesting stories that dotted the sports media landscape this week:

Without the possibility of a Dodgers-Red Sox World Series matchup -- Hollywood can only do so much to make everything a reality -- the L.A.-based Fox Sports executive think tank will say they're fine and dandy with the Phillies playing anyone for the title.

Even if it's the non-devilish Rays of Tampa Bay?

"You'll always find matchups on paper are going to look more attractive," Fox Sports boss Ed Goren said of a non-Dodger, non-Red Sox World Series next week. "We're going to play the hand we're given."

While baseball analysts like ESPN's Tim Kurkjian (talking on a recent "Pardon the Interruption") echoed what many fear, that Fox has a "ratings disaster" on its hands, Sports Illustrated's Tom Verducci, who did dugout reports on the ALCS during TBS' coverage, has another more pertinent angle.

"Adding to the low-profile problem (of Phillies-Rays) is the playoff schedule with the extra days off," he said. "We might be looking at as many as five straight days with no baseball by the time we get to World Series Game 1 (on Wednesday). By then, with any momentum killed, people will be out of the habit of tuning into baseball."

Wednesday's presidential debate didn't help Fox's Game 5 NLCS coverage.

Fox has persuaded MLB to push back the start of the World Series' potential Game 6 on Wednesday, Oct. 29, so it can run (along with CBS and NBC) a half-hour Barack Obama political commercial at 6 p.m..

Fans in the stadium waiting at the game now guaranteed to be on the East Coast should embrace that kind of delay (first pitch is now 8:35 p.m., rather than 8:22 p.m.), so the benefit is no pregame, while Fox makes about $1 million in the deal for selling the time.

One of the first reactions: "It's unfortunate the World Series' first pitch is being delayed for Obama's political pitch. Not only is Obama putting politics before principle, he's putting it before our national pastime."

That came from Republican National Committee spokesperson Alex Conant.

He must have conveniently forgotten that NBC moved the kickoff of the Sept. 4 Thursday night 2008 season opener between the Redskins and Giants up a full hour so it would finish before John McCain's Republican Convention acceptance speech.

Magic Johnson, ending a seven-season run as one of the lucky charms in the popular TNT NBA studio cereal bowl, said this week that the reason he'll be joining the ABC studio show starting on Christmas Day is the opportunity to cover the league finals.

These days, ABC and ESPN are interchangeable, all under the Disney multi-synergistic umbrella.

Which, if you think about it, why wouldn't the Magic Kingdom want a little more Magic in its kingdom?

John Madden isn't making the cross-country cruise in his custom-made bus from the homestead in Northern

California to Tampa, Fla., for Sunday's NBC NFL game between the Seahawks and Buccaneers.

Is he burned out? Could this be the first step toward him stepping aside? Who in NBC ever-expanding studio lineup could possibly be his successor?

For Sunday, it'll be Cris Collinsworth sitting in with Al Michaels (who, with wife Linda, got some primetime face time sitting in the primo Dodger Stadium seats next to the Dodgers' on-deck circle for the NLCS Game 5, after Pat Sajak and Penny Marshall vacated).

NBC also takes the next Sunday off, deferring to Game 3 of the World Series on Fox.

In essence, the 72-year-old Madden has a personal bye week, followed by a network bye. But don't say bye-bye so fast.

"Last spring when I got the (NFL) schedule I began to look ahead, and with this being a Super Bowl year, I wanted to try to get John a break," said NBC Sports chief Dick Ebersol. "I am simply looking to rest my best player, and I think this is part of being a smart manager and nothing more."

Said Madden, who blames the lack of success by the local San Francisco 49ers and Oakland Raiders for not giving him a "home" game broadcast once and awhile: "I enjoy the travel, but I have five grandkids and don't get home sometimes for five or six months."

If you want to read more into it, sure, NBC has to think ahead. So does Madden.

Again, if you're looking for clues on who'll replace whom, consider that Madden asked Collinsworth to be the voice of the "Madden '09" video game instead of doing it himself.

Letters To The Editor

Vote no on narcissism

Narcissism, a personality disorder, is unfortunately in abundance throughout American politics. Does your politician have inflated self-esteem or grandiosity, a preoccupation with success and power, believe he/she is special and unique, require excessive admiration, have a sense of entitlement, take advantage of others to achieve his or her agenda, lack empathy, believe that others are envious of him/her, show arrogance, haughty behaviors or attitudes?

If so, your politician is a narcissist who is more interested in their personal needs than their constituents'. This behavior contributes to gridlock and an ineffective Congress and violates rules of common decency that regular citizens utilize in their daily lives to be productive at work, at home, and with friends. If we carried ourselves as some of our politicians do on Capitol Hill, our lives would reflect the train wreck that is American politics.

You can do something about it - at the next election - vote them out.

Chip Melick, Denver

Amendment 52 will benefit roads and bridges As someone who is tired of watching politicians dither while our roads and bridges fall into disrepair, I'm voting for Amendment 52. It is a smart plan that reinvests extra severance tax dollars in better roads and safer bridges - with no tax increase for Colorado taxpayers. Yes, Amendment 52 puts road funding in the state constitution, but a portion of existing road funding (the Highway Users Trust Fund) is already in the constitution. Of course, there is a reason the drafters of Amendment 52 put the money in the constitution. Each and every year the state legislature raids hundreds of millions of dollars from transportation funding. Enough is enough. It is time to fix our roads and bridges.

I agree with all the business leaders who have endorsed Amendment 52. Our transportation system is our single biggest asset in the state and belongs to all of us. We must make sure it is maintained and safe. It is time to get to work on Colorado's ailing infrastructure. Join me and vote "yes" on Amendment 52.

Terri Binder, Grand Junction

Sally Alberts, Monument

This letter demonstrates how negative ads impact the opinion of people. If the writer had read the article in The Denver Post (page 23A) on Oct 12. titled "Web ad from McCain is dark, sinister, and a big pack of lies," she would have learned that the Chicago Annenberg Challenge is an educational foundation created by a mainstream Republican. William Ayers, now a college professor, "was active in getting the foundation started but was never on the board."

Obama has expressed his disapproval of Ayers' past actions and violent history. I doubt that anyone would want to be held responsible for the actions of people we've known through our lives.

And what the writer sees as Obama's elitism, I see as intelligence, a trait that this country desperately needs in the Oval Office.

Marie Ungemah, Littleton

Apples and oranges

I received this e-mail from my daughter regarding my 13-year-old grandson's take on last week's presidential debate:

"He watched some of the debate last night. This morning he told me that, to him, it was like they were asked if they like apples or oranges better and they replied that they like cheese on their burgers."

How could it possibly be described any better?

Johnie Morris, Littleton

----------------------------------------

Does the public really need "public opinion"? Re: "Arapahoe political tide turns," Oct. 9 news story.

The news media too often subject us to the results of one poll or another. Apparently they believe that we readers and viewers cannot formulate decisions; rather, we must be instructed about "public opinion."

But consider how illogical that assumption is. If we need "public opinion" to guide our thought and yet we are fools, who is that "public" whose opinion should make decisions for us?

Your Oct. 9 article revealed that Floyd Ciruli, founder of the oft-quoted poll-takers Ciruli Associates, is former chairman of the Colorado Democratic Party. Now there's an objective poll-taker!

We are all aware that a question's form profoundly affects the likely answer. For example, "Do you think schools should be adequately funded?" is a far different question than "Do you support raising taxes to increase school administrators' salaries?"

News reports of public-opinion polls derive from the insulting premise that we cannot decide issues for ourselves. Topping that fundamental insult with the choice of a politically motivated pollster reflects poor judgment or a crude effort to mold our

choices.

We deserve greater respect.

Peg Brady, Centennial

----------------------------------------

Better way to pick VP

Am I the only one in the United States who is upset about the way the vice president is picked? The presidential candidates, who won their nomination by primaries, get to pick anyone to run for vice president. Several times in my life, a vice president has been called on to serve. I feel that the vice presidential candidate should also be by the will of the people. The runner-up in the primary vote should be vice president.

The vice president doesn't have to be a buddy to the president. But he or she should be by the will of the people, not the will of the presidential candidate.

Charlotte Heath, Montrose

FINAL EDITION


A good night for whiskey


BYLINE: Ed Quillen Denver Post Columnist


SECTION: PERSPECTIVE; Pg. D-04


LENGTH: 583 words


We don't always sip scotch whisky at the Salida Single Malt Sampling Society. We also taste rye, tequila, Irish, bourbon - and last week, the only known legal Colorado whiskey, 92-proof Stranahan's. Our verdict was that it was pretty rough on the first sip, but seemed to improve as one moved down the glass.

We could not be sure, however, whether that was the nature of the potation or a variant of how "the girls always look prettier at closing time." Our closing time was early so we could go home and watch the presidential debate.

I'm not sure why we bothered. For one thing, I don't know why we call these events "debates." I took the course in high school. There was a formal proposition, and we took the affirmative or negative. We had to address the proposition, rather than mean-mouth the opposition.

The best part about the class was that often, you didn't know which side you'd be on until class started, so you had to learn both sides and be prepared to argue either.

A real debate between candidates would have a proposition like "Resolved: The United States should adopt a universal single-payer health-care system" or "Resolved: Alaska should be an independent nation." The candidates would make their arguments and rebuttals, and we would judge accordingly.

The alleged model for the modern presentations is the series of debates between Stephen A. Douglas and Abraham Lincoln for an Illinois U.S. Senate seat in 1858. The candidates alternated as to who would speak first. The first speaker got an hour, the second an hour and a half, and the first speaker came back for 30 minutes of rebuttal, for a total of three hours.

What we have now is more like a public job interview, with applicants fielding and evading questions. Why do we call it a debate?

And where do the networks find those "undecided" voters to query? Both candidates have been running hard for more than a year. Anyone with the slightest interest in public affairs has all the information necessary to make a decision, and there can't be that many Americans who just emerged from comas.

But wait. Might some candidate make a game-changing gaffe? I'll wager that Barack Obama lost not a single vote when he said "green behind the ears" instead of "wet behind the ears," and John McCain's "that one" did not change anyone's mind.

It turned out to be an evening better devoted for sampling whiskey, but perhaps the final "debate" this week will be an improvement. The odds are against it, though. Whiskey gets better with age, but campaigns apparently do not.

In last Sunday's column, I wrote that John Marshall, manager of two losing Republican campaigns, became development director at Mesa State College from a patronage appointment, and the job was not advertised.

One source was an article in the Grand Junction Free Press, which quoted Mesa President Tim Foster saying the college chose not to advertise the position because it wanted to act quickly to continue raising money to remodel the fieldhouse.

This inspired a call from Foster, followed by an e-mail. He said the college does not have patronage jobs and normal hiring procedures were followed; the position was advertised on the college's website and in local media.

"I also appreciate the retraction - if it comes - will, in all likelihood, be marginalized," Foster wrote.

Well, I've done my best to hide it here.

Ed Quillen (ed@cozine.com) is a freelance writer, history buff, publisher of Colorado Central Magazine in Salida and frequent contributor to The Post.

October 12, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


THE EXTRA MILE CAMPAIGN 2008 | With Obama and McCain virtually tied in Colorado and 23 days until the election, supporters scramble near and far to swing last-minute votes.


BYLINE: Allison Sherry The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-01


LENGTH: 1428 words


SALIDA

- On a patio overlooking the sparkling Arkansas River, Sen. Ken Salazar, wearing his custom white ten-gallon hat and bolo tie, stood on a chair and began his pitch to a hundred or so people gathered at a Mexican restaurant.

"Barack Obama wants everyone in this state to understand that ... no matter how far you are off the beaten path ... you count," Salazar said. "We are here to celebrate Chaffee County and to let you know how important you are to Barack Obama."

The half-hour stop ended with campaign staffers jokingly telling the chips-and-salsa-eating crowd that they held the rally in a room with one exit so they could snag volunteers when people left.

The night before, a half-dozen "Hispanics for McCain" canvassed a Pueblo neighborhood after work. Harold Trujillo chatted about Arizona's Sen. John McCain and running mate Sarah Palin on front porches in the traditional Democratic stronghold.

"I'm going to die a Democrat, but he (McCain) was there for me," said Trujillo, a 66-year-old retired steelworker. "He was out fighting for my freedom, so he deserves my vote."

Welcome to a raging battleground state 23 days out from a national election, where the push to find the roughly 8 percent of undecided voters here has grown into a fierce 24-hour-a-day foot race between the parties, which vow they need Colorado's nine electoral votes to win the White House.

To understand the candidates' political field strategy in a swing state is to understand some of the most scientific approaches that exist in unearthing wavering voters and pulling them over the fence.

The campaigns are wary about giving specifics, but a week of shadowing door-knockers, phone banks and outreach efforts on both sides revealed bare-knuckled aggression to woo voters from Denver to Sterling to Pueblo to Montrose.

McCain and Obama campaign strategists have created intelligence reports of sorts on voters, neighborhoods and cities.

Using formulas parsing voter history, the kinds of magazines people read, cars they drive, credit cards they own and jobs they hold, they have mapped tossup precincts, tossup homes and tossup voters.

They look at neighborhoods, cities and counties that have seen demographic shifts. A Democrat or a Republican who has always been a loyal party voter may get a call from the other side based on where they just moved or a newspaper they just started subscribing to.

"In the final three weeks, we only have the capacity to talk to so many voters," said Anne Filipic, Obama's general election director for Colorado. "The last thing you want to do is limit your campaign."

Volunteers are sent to walk blocks with campaign literature and clipboards, trying to find undecided voters and "base" people who may not be particularly inclined to vote.

This second group is important because they could be a shoo-in for a particular party - they just need to be goaded to the polls, said Ken Smukler, president of InfoVoter Technologies, a Philadelphia-based political consulting firm.

"If you polled the African-American community, it's probably 95 to 5 percent for Obama," he said. "The problem isn't convincing an African-

American voter how to vote; the problem is convincing a voter who hasn't voted before to get out to the poll."

In these targeted areas, people are so weary of campaign volunteers at their doorsteps, residents often no longer answer their doors.

Stuck on the porch

On a walk through a southwest Denver neighborhood last week, Obama volunteer 26-year-old Raj Grewal was having little luck - even at homes with cars in the driveway. Most front porches were cluttered with ballot initiative and U.S. Senate race fliers.

A New York corporate lawyer who took a sabbatical to volunteer for Obama in Colorado, he received only one response: A women yelled from the door that she was too busy.

When campaigns locate undecided voters, they usually try to find a volunteer who looks like them to make the pitch.

McCain spokesman Tom Kise said an undecided registered Democrat may get an automated call from someone like Joe Lieberman, the Connecticut Democrat turned independent who is supporting McCain.

"If they're in Pueblo and they're Hispanic, maybe (local Latino activist) Silver Salazar calls them," Kise said.

The 9 percent to 10 percent of undecided voters nationwide are likely just starting to pay attention to the campaigns, political experts say. They may turn on a debate or turn off a negative commercial. They aren't tuning in to day-to-day stump speeches or gotcha snarks employed by both parties.

"There's not much pie left to split," said former U.S. Rep. Scott McInnis, now a Denver lawyer advising the McCain campaign. "You're running out of time to change the direction of the ship. ... Are we better to be at soccer games, or are we better to focus on a CU football game? It comes to down to decisions."

And as the campaign season draws down, those pounding the pavement often become that lever that turns the election results that crucial 1 or 2 percentage points to victory.

Personal appeals crucial

"In the battleground states, it's critical," Smukler said. "In a race that is cutting by a half a percentage point, it's not the TV ads, it's the ground operations, it's the grassroots organizations. ... That's where it matters."

Enter former Gov. Roy Romer, plunking himself down in a booth at a Pueblo greasy spoon, bending the ear of an undecided voter eating breakfast. Or Sam Sanchez in Pueblo, who walked a neighborhood last week for McCain after his shift ended at the Target distribution center.

Silver Salazar spends most days in his "Democrats for McCain" T-shirt in the basement headquarters of the Republican hopeful's field office in Pueblo. A cousin to Sen. Salazar and a prominent Pueblo political activist, he is helping reach out to Latino voters.

He said the undecided, mostly

middle-class Latino voters need to hear how McCain relates to them.

"It's sort of like, who can help me the most?" Salazar said, noting his wife is undecided. "It's about forming coalitions, Hispanics for McCain, Veterans for McCain, Women for McCain. ... It's talking to people about who is the best person to represent them."

At a McCain phone bank in Centennial recently, more than 25 people filled the room after work - toting children, pets and pizza - to make calls to targeted voters. When volunteers reached someone voting for McCain, they would ring a bell.

"Obviously if it were a slam dunk in Colorado, you wouldn't feel half as inclined to spend your time in here rather than a golf course," said Lowell Arnold, a 77-year-old retired insurance consultant, working the phones. "Every vote counts in Colorado."

The newcomer factor

The state isn't accustomed to being so hotly contested. It voted for a Democrat in the presidential election only once in the past 40 years: In 1992, Colorado went for Bill Clinton, but Ross Perot took 23 percent of the vote that year, cutting a large swath into incumbent President George Bush's base.

But most polls, including one completed by The Denver Post at the beginning of October, put Obama and McCain in a statistical tie here. Both campaigns say they are expanding their volunteer armies as Election Day nears.

State observers say Colorado's entrenched political right-leaning loyalties are working against a large number of educated newcomers to the mostly urban Front Range who are proud to be unpredictable and independent.

Sen. Salazar said these people are "tired of ideology and division. ... They care about jobs, health care and energy overdependence."

Rural roots strong

But not forgotten in either campaign are the state's rural roots, and the voters in outlying areas who are usually more fiscally and socially conservative.

In Ken Salazar's swing through these areas last week on behalf of Obama, he affirmed that the Illinois senator wouldn't take away their guns and that he believed in protecting wildlands for hunters and fishers.

Valerie Harris and Guy Hummel, who live outside Pueblo, sat at a diner while Romer and the Salazar brothers, Sen. Salazar and U.S. Rep. John Salazar, gladhanded people eating huevos rancheros and biscuits and gravy.

The brother and sister pair run a small cattle ranch and are struggling with the recent financial crisis because they can't get a short-term loan from the local Pueblo bank to buy more cattle.

"We care about water, health care and the economy," said Harris, watching Obama staffers set up a podium near their booth. She and her brother are undecided. "It's hard for us here. ... We need someone to understand."

Allison Sherry: 303-954-1377 or asherry@denverpost.com

FINAL EDITION


But he's a maverick


BYLINE: David Harsanyi


SECTION: Pg. B-11


LENGTH: 632 words


This election has never been about John McCain - though his candidacy is sure to revive a debate about the worst presidential candidates of all time.

No, this is a referendum on Barack Obama. And many Republicans are exuding the confidence of a hopelessly quixotic sports fan - a person who watches his atrocious team struggle for three quarters with the false expectation that some miraculous comeback is imminent in the fourth.

It rarely is.

McCain has consistently remained inconsistent, vacillating between promises and populism. From his support of cap-and-trade to his actions during the bailout, McCain's positions seem entirely focused on winning the middle-

of-the-road vote.

No modern Republican has ever won the presidency solely focused on the ambivalent squishy inattentive center. These people don't care enough to name their political party, much less pay attention.

But he's a maverick. One of McCain's central arguments has been his uncompromising valor in opposing the Bush administration.

Here's a newsbreak: Disagreeing with the Bush administration on a handful of issues (often the wrong ones, in McCain's case) doesn't make you a maverick, it makes you an average American. And, sadly, the second debate proved that McCain would be incapable of making his party's philosophical or political case even if he genuinely tried.

When Obama claims, as he did during the second debate, that raising taxes on the rich is the equivalent of giving them a "break," McCain, terrified of defending anyone in a Lexus, failed to make an assertive case that economic freedom helps everyone.

When Obama claimed he would lower income taxes for 95 percent of Americans even though nearly 40 percent of households don't pay a single cent in income taxes, McCain just smiled.

When Obama continued his absurd insistence that our financial mess was caused only by Bush-era "deregulation," McCain struggled to place the blame where it belongs: on government meddling.

And when Obama contended his economic plan would be a "net" cut in spending, McCain should have spit the water out of his mouth like a character on a TV sitcom - because that's exactly what the senior senator from Arizona is starring in.

Those Republicans anticipating a fourth-quarter comeback during the debate were instead hit with a wet fish. Did the putative Republican candidate just propose that the U.S. Treasury renegotiate millions of mortgages at a better price?

Was McCain simply unable to articulate a more complex position? It sounded a lot like a comprehensive nationalization of the mortgage industry. It sounded a lot like hundreds of billions of additional tax dollars.

Yep, he meant it. It's called the American Homeownership Resurgence. It will stabilize the economy. And Obama will stop global warming. And McCain will find bin Laden, even if he has to do it with his bare hands. And ... well, at this pace, we're about two debates away from being promised free lemonade and snicker doodles.

None of these promises have worked. So now the McCain campaign will set its sights on Bill Ayers, Rev. Jeremiah Wright, Tony Rezko and other members of the Legion of Doom. All of them are legitimate topics for conversation, but with less than a month to go, the conversation reeks of desperation.

In fact, the entire campaign has been one big act of publicity stunts. McCain's shining moment this campaign, as far as I can tell, was a funny ad comparing Obama to Paris Hilton.

What McCain's candidacy does tell us is that the Republican Party - even if it somehow miraculously pulls this one out - is in need of some creative destruction. Not ideological purity but ideological renewal.

Because being a "maverick" is a political slogan, not a political philosophy.

Reach columnist David Harsanyi at 303-954-1255 or dharsanyi@denverpost.com

FINAL EDITION


Campaign 2008 >> Second presidential debate Slinging, sympathizing


BYLINE: Karen E. Crummy The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-01


LENGTH: 818 words


NASHVILLE, TENN. - John McCain and Barack Obama drew sharp contrasts on taxes, the economy and foreign policy Tuesday as the two battled to capture both the mantle of reformer and the votes of undecided Americans in a town-

hall-style debate.

In the shadow of another disastrous day on Wall Street and an increasingly nasty race, the two presidential candidates clashed on nearly every question asked by moderator Tom Brokaw and a handful of undecided voters at Belmont University. Yet, most of their comments were cautious and well-scripted lines from the campaign trail.

"There wasn't much new from the past debate, but they did present voters with clear contrasts," said Diana B. Carlin, a professor of communication studies at the University of Kansas who has surveyed political debates for more than 20 years. "They had very different philosophies, and as an undecided voter, if you were looking for something particular, you probably found it."

Despite the trash-talking that led up to the second presidential debate, the candidates generally steered clear of character attacks and instead slapped each other's records. Although both tried to reach out to the audience, the debate was devoid of any remarkable events like the "I feel your pain" moment by Bill Clinton in 1992.

Republican McCain, fighting to gain momentum in the wake of national polls that show him trailing Democrat Obama, proposed to help homeowners with a $300 billion federal cash infusion.

"I would order the secretary of the Treasury to immediately buy up the bad home loan mortgages in America and renegotiate at the new value of those homes, at the diminished value of those homes, and let people make those, be able to make those payments and stay in their homes. Is it expensive? Yes," he said. (His campaign said the money would be part of the $700 billion "rescue" plan.)

Obama, who often focused on the middle class, stressed his tax-cut plan for those making under $250,000 a year.

"It means help for homeowners so that they can stay in their homes," he said. "It means that we are helping state and local governments set up road projects and bridge projects that keep people in their jobs. And then long term we've got to fix our health care system, and we've got to fix our energy system that is putting such an enormous burden on families."

The 90-minute debate came 28 days before Election Day. About 80 people, picked by the independent Gallup Organization, attended the event. About one-third was undecided, another third leaned toward McCain and the final third was shifting toward Obama.

Both candidates agreed on a gloomy forecast for Wall Street and Main Street but were optimistic that the right leader could help pull the economy up by its bootstraps.

McCain blamed the mortgage crisis on Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, as well as Obama's "cronies and friends" there. Obama said the economic meltdown was the "final verdict" on the Bush administration's failed economic policies, as well as McCain's backing of deregulation.

McCain painted Obama as inexperienced and naive on foreign policy and a hide-the-ball, tax-and-spend liberal.

"Nailing down Sen. Obama's various tax proposals is like nailing Jell-O to the wall," McCain said.

Obama characterized McCain as erratic and a continuation of the Bush administration. He also criticized McCain for possessing the "wrong judgment" on foreign-policy issues, such as the war in Iraq, and stretching the truth not only about Obama's record but his own.

"I think the 'Straight Talk Express' lost a wheel on that one," Obama said, referring to the way McCain construed Obama's tax plan.

See-sawing between sitting on stools and walking around the horseshoe-shaped stage, McCain and Obama were generally polite, but at times could not hide their annoyance with one another.

Immediately after being introduced, McCain said to Obama: "It's good to be with you at a town-hall meeting," a swipe at Obama for rejecting the Arizona's senator's request to hold joint town halls during the election.

He later referred to Obama as "that one."

As the two sparred on foreign policy, McCain said he believes in Theodore Roosevelt's mantra of "speak softly and carry a big stick."

Obama retorted: "This is a guy who sang bomb, bomb, bomb Iran, who called for the annihilation of North Korea - that I don't think is an example of speaking softly."

Going into the second to last debate, the candidates had different objectives and challenges. Obama, usually more comfortable in large venues, needed to exhibit a more emotional, personal touch with the goal of solidifying his national lead. McCain, who excels in small town-hall events, wanted to reverse the damage he has sustained over the past few weeks, while not coming across as angry.

Carlin, the Kansas professor, said she thought both candidates did what they had to do, though ``they can't really do anything radically different at this stage.''

October 5, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


Many undecided voters don't tune in until late Unaffiliated Colorado voters favor Obama in a new poll, but plenty stay on the fence.


BYLINE: Allison Sherry The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-20


LENGTH: 495 words


If John McCain or Barack Obama wants to win the vote of 58-year-old small-business owner Gary Suposs, he should take note of the typical composite of an undecided voter.

Suposs, with his two jobs, hasn't had a lot of time to pay attention to the daily back-and-forth snarks coming from either side.

As an equipment company business owner and farmer in Paonia, he worries about the economy. He is dubious about the $700 billion financial bailout but doesn't completely understand it. He wants to know how the United States is going to get out of Iraq, and he wants to hear what both candidates are going to do for him and his small business.

He is waiting for the next two debates, which he's going to put his life aside for, to make his decision.

"The rest of this stuff," he said, railing against the negative commercials, "sometimes I want to throw a brick through the television."

In the latest Denver Post poll, unaffiliated voters in Colorado favor Obama, 49 percent to McCain's 31 percent. Yet another key 11 percent say they are still undecided in Colorado. The poll's margin of error among unaffiliated voters is plus or minus 5 percentage points.

Among all voters, however, the two candidates are deadlocked here. McCain offsets Obama's sizeable lead among unaffiliateds because he does much better among registered Republicans, according to the poll.

Usually undecided voters are too busy to pay attention until the month before the election, experts say.

"These voters haven't tuned in fully," said Vanderbilt University political scientist John Geer.

And if they do, they resist a party label. They usually don't align themselves with hardcore values of any kind - Western, conservative or liberal - which means they are hard-pressed to be blown away by a standard stump speech.

"There's less regionalism than there used to be," said University of Colorado at Boulder professor Ken Bickers. "Most of the people who vote in Colorado aren't ranchers, they aren't rural, they're not tied to water issues in a direct way. ... They are linked to the national and global economy."

Statewide, the poll showed that the economy is by far the No. 1 issue. Worries about money and jobs overtook the war as the top concern last spring, but the economy is now almost the single issue, with 77 percent of Coloradans naming it. No other concern received more than 5 percent attention.

Take 55-year-old Frances Kelly, who lives in a rural outskirt town near Lamar. She is worried about the economy, the war and the national debt.

Though she doesn't have an affinity for all ideas Democratic, she's decided to vote for Obama for her grandchildren.

"I don't know what kind of a world we're leaving them," she said.

Bickers said McCain needs to try to find a way to connect with independents - particularly with the economic downturn.

"This is not McCain's election to lose," Bickers said. "It's McCain's election to try and find a way to win."

Allison Sherry: 303-954-1377 or asherry@denverpost.com

Insurance

AIG plans sale of business units to repay debt

The insurer American International Group Inc. said Friday it plans to sell off a number of business units to pay off its massive government loan.

The announcement was expected by Wall Street. But it now leaves investors wondering how much AIG will be able to raise from the sales.

On the brink of failure last month, AIG was bailed out when the government offered it an $85 billion loan during the ongoing credit crisis that saw Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. file for bankruptcy protection and the sale of Merrill Lynch & Co. to Bank of America Corp. In return for the loan, the government received warrants to purchase up to 79.9 percent of AIG.

Autos

Toyota's zero-percent offer shows credit is available

Toyota Motor Corp.'s unprecedented offer of zero-percent financing on nearly a dozen models is trying to make the point that tight credit is no excuse for buyers -- in fact, it's literally giving credit away for free.

But after the top Japanese automaker posted a 32 percent drop in September sales that only domestic competitors had experienced until now, the question is: Is it enough to get people to buy?

Toyota's U.S. division said Thursday night it will offer the free financing on 11 models with terms ranging from 36 to 60 months. The incentive expires Nov. 3.

Employment

Economy sheds most jobs since 2003, more cuts seen

Jobs are vanishing at the fastest pace in more than five years with pink slips likely to keep stacking higher in the months ahead, an urgent signal the country may be careening toward a deep and painful recession just as Americans prepare to elect a new president.

Whether that's Democrat Barack Obama or Republican John McCain, one of them will be dealing with the weakest employment climate in years.

Increasingly skittish employers dropped the ax even harder in September, chopping payrolls by 159,000 -- more than double the cuts made just one month before. It was the ninth straight month of job losses. A staggering 760,000 jobs have disappeared so far this year.

Seminars

Camera hosting Web sessions for local businesses

The Camera is hosting a three-session seminar this month led by Web consultant Libby Niemi to help local businesses learn how to maximize their Web presence.

The seminars are:

Search Engine Optimization

-- Learn how to easily navigate the complicated algorithms that affect your site's search engine return rankings -- 9 a.m. Oct. 14 or 3 p.m. Oct. 21.

Search Engine Marketing

-- Learn how to make the most of your word selections, ad copy and Web site landing page to strategically increase your web traffic -- 3 p.m. Oct. 15 or 9 a.m. Oct. 22 .

E-mail Marketing

-- Learn how to get started with this proven method for increasing customer loyalty, retention and your bottom line -- 9 a.m. Oct. 16 or 3 p.m. Oct. 23.

All sessions will be held at the Camera, 1048 Pearl St., Boulder. Seminars are $50 per class or $100 for all three sessions. Light snacks and parking provided.
