The Denver Post


August 24, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


A new Democratic revolution Changing demographics transform a party and place it on the edge of opportunity


BYLINE: Stories by John Aloysius Farrell , Special to The Denver Post


SECTION: Pg. S-02


LENGTH: 4070 words


Each presidential election tells a story: a sprawling American saga as big as the republic. There are ideological revolutions and moments of generational change. There are eras of lazy choices. And there are times of peril when transformative public figures seize the national stage. As the first of the year's great political conventions begins, it is impossible to say whether John McCain or Barack Obama will emerge victorious in November. Yet it is undeniable that it is the Democratic Party that now faces the harrowing challenge and sublime prize of historic opportunity.

By their own admission, the Republicans are in

trouble.

Americans are upset over an uncertain economy, soaring food and energy prices, an interminable war, and declining incomes. A record number of citizens, according to the polls, think the country is on the wrong track, and a record number believe the Republican president is doing a poor job. In the past nine months, the Democrats have registered millions of new voters and won three off-year congressional elections on hostile turf in the South and Midwest.

"The Democrats are clearly winning the enthusiasm game, certainly winning the turnout contest, winning the money game and winning the congressional recruitment game," says Karlyn Bowman, an expert on polling at the American Enterprise Institute, a conservative think tank in Washington, D.C.

From the White House and Congress to the statehouses and city halls, the 2008 campaign appears to be the Democrats' to lose.

For 40 years, they have struggled to cope on a political landscape that has favored their opponents in a right-leaning country. Just once in the past 10 presidential elections has the Democratic candidate gotten more than 50 percent of the popular vote, and that was Jimmy Carter, who won 50.08 percent in 1976 in the wake of the Watergate scandal.

In that time, the Democrats lost their mastery of Congress, the presidency and the Supreme Court. Their Southern base joined the other side. The once-proud label - liberal - became a political epithet. Worst of all, as it struggled to cope, the party lost its brand.

"Most Americans have no clue what Democrats stand for," wrote Al From and Bruce Reed of the Democratic Leadership Council in a postmortem review of their party's defeat in the 2004 presidential election.

The Democratic Party no longer was seen as the defender of the little guy, but rather as a voice for elitists who undermined American families with confiscatory taxes, social engineering, loose moral discipline, over-

regulation and timidity abroad.

"It feels like we're being judged or pounded down on because we want to carry a gun or because we want to wear the American flag pin," says Josh Williams, 24, an independent voter from Charlottesville, Va., who has been turned off by what he perceives as Democratic elitism.

Conservatives increasingly referred to the United States as a "center-right" nation, and few historians argued with them. The last three Democratic presidencies were marred by failure, and the most recent moments of Democratic genius - Franklin D. Roosevelt's New Deal, Woodrow Wilson's first term - have receded from memory. For anyone younger than a baby boomer, the scandal-plagued Clinton terms are all they know of a Democratic presidency.

Yet in the course of those 40 years, America continued to change. The ideological choices the Democrats made in the 1960s and '70s, which then seemed like prescriptions for social disorder, now look to many like templates for American success in a global, postindustrial economy. The values prized by Democrats - tolerance, diversity, personal liberty, unfettered scientific inquiry, public education, environmental stewardship, equal rights for women, a social insurance net and a cooperative foreign policy - have been embraced by millions of Americans, especially by younger generations, as a program for the future.

Demographically, a more youthful, educated and multi-hued electorate appears poised to put its stamp on American history and seems to like what the Democrats are selling.

"I see, on my side of the aisle, a party that is very much at variance with just about every major trend going on in America today," says David Frum, a Republican analyst and former speechwriter for President Bush. "It is hard to look at these numbers without seeing flashing red warning indicators that Republicanism as we know it, as an ideology and as a party system, is in tremendous danger, if not already obsolete."

The 2006 election offered proof. Democratic House and Senate candidates built formidable campaign treasuries and identified the party's highly motivated troops and got them to the polls. Independents and other swing voters, aghast at the course of the Iraq war and the Bush administration's fumbling response to Hurricane Katrina, voted for change.

When the counting was done, the Democrats had picked up 30 seats in the House, six governor's mansions and six seats in the Senate. They seized control of Congress for the first time since 1994.

The new Democratic coalition

Who is in the Democratic coalition these days?

According to television exit polls, Democratic candidates beat their Republican foes in the 2006 congressional elections by 10 points or better among women, union members, Americans younger than 30, those with postgraduate degrees, African-Americans, Latinos, voters who earn less than $100,000, liberals and moderates, political independents, gay and lesbian voters, Catholics and Jews, and secular-minded voters who don't regularly attend church.

Some of the groups the Democrats score well with - minorities, single and college-educated women, young people and professionals - are emerging as major components of the American electorate.

There are now four "majority-minority" states, where the combined minority population is larger than the number of white residents. The list includes two political giants, Texas and California, as well as New Mexico and Hawaii.

The U.S. Census Bureau estimates that five more states will join that category in the next two decades: Florida, Georgia, Arizona, Nevada and Maryland. Together, those nine states supply 165 of the 270 Electoral College votes needed for victory.

The Democratic Party scored well with many of these minority groups in 2006. Democrats won 89 percent of the African-American vote, 69 percent of the Latino vote and 62 percent of Asian-American voters.

And perhaps most significantly, the Democrats won 60 percent of the voters younger than 30 in 2006. The party has capitalized on the excitement stirred by the Hillary Rodham Clinton and Obama campaigns to register tens of thousands of additional young voters this year.

Research shows that each generation forges its political identity in its youth and generally maintains it for a lifetime. And "this has not been a good time for Republicans," said Scott Keeter, a public-opinion expert at the Pew Research Center for the People & the Press in Washington, D.C. The war in Iraq and troubles at home "have hurt the Republican brand among young people."

The 2008 presidential primaries offered a telling contrast, says Democratic Party chairman Howard Dean. The all-white, all-male Republican contenders "look like the 1950s." The Democrats, with a Latino, a woman and a victorious African-American candidates, "look like the rest of America," Dean says. "We are the party of America's future."

A revolution in progress

Yet "the emerging Democratic majority" remains a blueprint at this point. It is a revolution yet to be born.

The Republican and Democratic presidential candidates are "bookends of the demographic transformation we are going to be encountering in this country," says William Frey, a professor at the University of Michigan's Institute for Social Research in Ann Arbor. "This is what McCain and Obama reflect: the past and the future.

"The trick is," Frey says, "we are somewhere in the middle right now."

Obama will be facing McCain in November in a country in which four out of five voters still describe themselves as moderate or conservative and the two largest demographic groups are white women (40 percent) and white men (39 percent).

In 2006, according to the exit polls, the typical American voter was white (79 percent), married (68 percent), Protestant (55 percent), heterosexual (97 percent) and without a postgraduate degree (72 percent).

To millions of Americans, the attributes that recommend Obama to Democrats are negatives. Where his admirers see "young" and "dare" and "hope" and "change," his detractors see "raw" and "untested" and "risk" and "strange."

"I am a Democrat, and I am thinking more about McCain, just because I don't trust Osama, I mean Obama - it's only one-letter difference. His middle name is Hussein," says Charles Fasano, 56, of York, Pa., who voted for John Kerry in 2004 and for Clinton in the state's primary this spring. "My feeling is he has done nothing to show America he is worthy of being the president. He has just recently come out to say, 'I am proud to be an American.' His wife is down on America. He is down on America. ... You know, he comes from a Muslim family. (Obama is a Christian, but his father and stepfather were raised in the Muslim faith.) I just don't - it's not right. I can't see it. I fear for America if he comes in."

Of all America's presidents, only one took the White House with less relevant experience than Barack Obama.

It was Abraham Lincoln.

The archetypical face of America's future may be a group of 20- and 30-somethings, of varying ethnicity, gender and marital status, checking their BlackBerrys in Austin, Texas, or Palo Alto, Calif., or using their Apple laptops to build profiles on Facebook or to check out YouTube via Wi-Fi in Seattle.

But as Clinton demonstrated in New Hampshire, Ohio and Pennsylvania, there is still a strong, more traditional American voice in both parties. These Americans are married, go to church and don't have advanced educational degrees. They live on the edges of the suburbs, or in midsize cities and towns in rural America, where housing costs are lower and the schools are safer. They take their sons hunting, join softball leagues, mourn for Dale Earnhardt and cheer for Peyton Manning.

The Democratic Party doesn't neglect the working class. On its behalf, and with its help, congressional Democrats fought and won two big legislative battles in Bush's second term: stopping the privatization of Social Security and raising the minimum wage.

Like Obama, many Democratic leaders have roots in working-class communities and earned, through hard work, their college degrees and fine salaries.

And the Democratic economic platform is a populist classic: to cut taxes on working-class and middle-class families, raise taxes on those who make more than $200,000 a year, and spend the money on roads, schools, health care and other keys to working- and middle-class opportunity.

"There has never been a period when voters are more determined to have a government that works for the common person rather than, as the one we have now, that works for special interests and the big corporations and the wealthy," says Democratic pollster Stan Greenberg.

And yet, Greenberg is forced to concede that it is a challenge for Democrats to carry working-class wards and precincts. In the past decade, blue-collar voters "have moved over to the Republican side because Democrats worked so hard to push them away," he says.

For a party whose leading candidates, donors and tacticians hail from Ivy League campuses, the great law and lobbying firms of Washington, and ritzy ZIP codes in Manhattan and Los Angeles in recent years, elitism is a recurring problem.

"It's the party of trial lawyers, labor bosses and big-

city political machines, and of the two wings of the dependency movement - the fellows locked into welfare and the fellows that make $90,000 a year managing the dependency of others," says conservative activist Grover Norquist, an ardent libertarian.

"And all the various, what I would call, Coercive Utopians: the guys who get government grants to fix the rest of us," Norquist says. "These are the people who make the cars too small to put entire families into and the toilets too small to completely flush."

When Al Gore was vice president, he seized the opportunity to "reinvent government" - smashing ashtrays on "The Late Show with David Letterman" to illustrate misguided procurement practices. And in contrast to what happened in New Orleans during Katrina, the Clinton administration's Federal Emergency Management Agency performed ably during such disasters as the Oklahoma City bombing, the Northridge, Calif., earthquake and the floods that hit the Midwest in 1993.

But the Democrats never followed through on their reform agenda. Shaken by scandal, Clinton sought advice from his pollsters and took to championing small, symbolic issues such as school uniforms. An opening closed. Despite the Bush administration's blunders in Iraq, in New Orleans and along the Mexican border, the Democrats are still seen as the architects and defenders of a bumbling federal state.

"The second Clinton term will be remembered as one giant missed opportunity," party strategists William Galston and Elaine Kamarck wrote in 2005.

Even when the Democrats produce strategists who understand the "little guy" - hello, James Carville and Paul Begala - they lose them to the glitz and bucks of media sideshows.

"We've forgotten how to speak to their hearts, as well as their heads," says Sen. Joe Biden, D-Del. "We have to remind average Americans we know their problems."

And, at least until the Dean and Obama presidential campaigns showed the staggering potential of raising money from small donors via the Internet, the Democrats grew dependent on fat-cat cash. In Congress, not surprisingly, many supported pro-business trade, liability and bankruptcy laws that conflicted with the interests of "the common man."

"Did we grow too cautious for a campaign check?" John Edwards asked his audiences during this year's primary campaign. His answer was "Yes."

Obama's long, tough haul

Even in 2006, saddled with an unpopular president, sex and corruption scandals in Congress, and stalemate in Iraq, Republican candidates whipped their Democratic rivals among white men (54 percent-44 percent), Protestants (54-44), evangelical Christians (58-41), Southerners (53-45) and Americans who attend church regularly (55-43).

And the GOP candidates finished at rough parity with the Democrats among huge, vital segments of the electorate: men, white women, voters older than 65, college graduates, white Catholics, married Americans, suburbanites and parents.

Organized and motivated, those groups vote in droves and give McCain and the Republicans a base on which to build their 2008 campaign.

Much was made of Obama's declaration, when addressing a group of California Democrats this spring, that "bitter" working-class Americans "cling" to God and guns and "antipathy" to foreigners and immigrants. It was an illustrative example of the liberal condescension that haunts his party.

But, earlier in those same remarks, Obama gave a sober appraisal of the long, tough haul he faces selling hope and "change you can believe in" this fall.

"In a lot of these communities in big, industrial states like Ohio and Pennsylvania, people have been beaten down so long (and) they feel so betrayed by government that when they hear a pitch that is premised on not being cynical about government, then a part of them just doesn't buy it," Obama said.

"And ... it's true that when it's delivered by a 46-year- old black man named Barack Obama, then that adds another layer of skepticism."

It was no accident, then, that after being rejected by the white working-class voters of Ohio and Pennsylvania this spring, Obama's campaign papered Kentucky with a pamphlet that proclaimed his Christian convictions and portrayed him at a pulpit, before a glowing cross. It told the story of the time when the candidate "felt a beckoning of the spirit and accepted Jesus Christ into his life."

Or that one of his first general-election campaign commercials was directed at voters in Colorado, Georgia, Indiana, North Carolina, Virginia, Alaska, Montana, Ohio, Nevada, Florida and other Bush states, hailing "the dignity that comes from work" and boasting of the time, as an Illinois legislator, when Obama "passed a law to move people from welfare to work" and "slashed the rolls by 80 percent."

Said From and Reed: "A party that has averaged 44.5 percent of the vote in the last 10 presidential elections and has only won a majority of the popular vote for president twice in six decades needs to start winning over some of the voters it is losing."

Looking ahead, the Democrats face an Electoral College that has a constitutional bias toward less populated states that traditionally lean Republican and in which Gore and Kerry failed, by one-state losses (Florida in 2000 and Ohio in 2004), to assemble the needed 270 votes for victory.

The Democrats made huge progress in the Electoral College under Bill Clinton. He shattered the Republican lock and captured California, which has since rested comfortably in the Democratic column. But they have been hurt by the Bush family's success in two other Sun Belt giants - Texas and Florida - that have sizable minority populations.

As the primaries ended, most Washington analysts proclaimed that the lists of secure and battleground states seemed relatively stable. Indeed, 34 of the 50 states have voted for the same party in the past four presidential elections.

Of all the potential Republican candidates, McCain may give the Democrats the toughest time in the Electoral College.

McCain's independence has always played well in New Hampshire, for instance, and he should be able to hold his native Arizona. His regional roots and attitudes may frustrate the Democratic Party's Western strategy and its hopes of winning New Mexico, Nevada or Colorado. And his record as a Navy pilot and Vietnam prisoner of war could boost his votes among veterans and help secure Virginia and the Carolinas.

If there is a surprise shake-up in the Electoral College, it may well take place below the Mason-Dixon Line, where the descendents of slaves could play a crucial role in determining the fate of the first African-

American presidential nominee.

McCain's maverick ways and secular attitudes could temper the enthusiasm of Southern evangelical voters, for whom the Democrats will make a determined pitch this year. In Southern states such as Virginia, Georgia and North Carolina, a sizable African-American constituency might join with women, transplanted Northerners and high-tech professionals to make things interesting. Even if Obama can't win those states, he could force Republicans to spend time and money in districts and precincts they have long taken for granted.

Democrats' dream candidate?

This kind of political slicing and dicing is, of course, what Obama is avowedly not about. He burst upon the national political scene at the 2004 Democratic convention with a thrilling speech and the memorable line, "There is no red America. There is no blue America. There is only the United States of America."

If the postpartisan, pragmatic Obama emerges as the Democratic candidate, given the lousy climate for Republicans, he could be tough to beat. Obama speaks eloquently of such traditional American cultural values as family, hard work, faith and responsibility. He embodies the American dream: a half-black child raised by a white single mom, whose smarts, hard work and drive took him to Columbia University, Harvard Law School, the U.S. Senate and the Democratic nomination. With audacity and hope, he has gotten closer to the presidency than anyone, ever, who is not a white male.

Like Tiger Woods or Oprah Winfrey, Obama has the ability to "transcend race," says Norm Ornstein, a political analyst at the American Enterprise Institute. "He's not out there running a campaign like Jesse Jackson or Al Sharpton. This is a campaign that says, 'Let's overcome the whole notion of race."'

And, though famously divided over its two historic candidacies - after 34 million ballots were cast, Clinton and Obama finished the primary season just 200,000 votes apart - the Democratic Party knows what it believes.

Obama, working with a Democratic Congress, says he will start withdrawing troops from Iraq, repeal the Bush tax cuts for upper-income Americans, adopt a less belligerent foreign policy, invest in alternative energy sources, address global warming, and make health care more accessible and affordable. He would also, if given the opportunity, try to stop the conservative drift of the U.S. Supreme Court.

The voters might be ready for that agenda. The security and values issues the Republicans have used to defeat Democratic challengers in past elections may be paling in the face of economic distress.

"I'm registered Republican ... and I considered myself Republican up until this point. Now I'm like, 'I don't know what I am,' " says Janell Mader, 32, a homemaker with a 4-year-old daughter from York, Pa. "I live a very comfortable life ... but I see a lot of other families who are deeply affected by the economy, and it is difficult for me to know good, honest, hard-working, intelligent college-educated people who are losing their job and unable to afford the basic necessities for their children.

"I think that for most of my life, my decisions have been made based on morals and family values and, like, that whole belief system that I've had instilled in me since birth," Mader said. "And now all of a sudden our country is just, like, turned upside down with all these economic issues that we, I, haven't encountered in my lifetime. And it is really making me second-guess."

But there is another Obama, just as there was another Michael Dukakis in 1988, another Al Gore in 2000 and another John Kerry in 2004. The Republicans are proven masters at defining the Democratic presidential nominee in unfavorable terms, and Obama's relative inexperience, dark skin and liberal beliefs will give them ample material with which to work.

Will Americans elect a black man president?

America's original sin is slavery. And for more than two centuries, the issue of race has consumed our economy, our politics, our law and literature, and our sports and entertainment. Now America will likely have its first man of color nominated for president by a major political party. And he is running on a platform that says race is irrelevant, that it is time to put this history behind us.

For the Democratic Party, it is a special moment, one for which it deserves special credit. When Lyndon Johnson signed the great civil-rights legislation of the 1960s, he told his aide Bill Moyers that the Democrats had "delivered the South to the Republican Party for a long time." Knowing the political cost, Johnson and the Democrats paid it anyway. Will 2008 be the year when "the long time" comes to an end? In a new, multiracial America, will the Democrats be rewarded for the courage they showed when doing the right thing on civil rights?

In 1944, the American and British military commanders, convinced that all the indicators favored their success, launched a daring strike through Holland to cross the Rhine River, outflank the Nazi defenses and bring a quick end to the war. The plan failed, as one officer said famously, because its final, and most important, goal was "a bridge too far."

Today, the indicators of political demography favor the Democrats. What is not so clear, the experts say, is just when the future arrives.

In choosing Barack Obama as its nominee, will the Democratic Party transform America? Or is his nomination a bridge too far?: -------------- Writer John Aloysius Farrell is the former Washington bureau chief for The Denver Post. He is currently working on a biography of legendary defense attorney Clarence Darrow.

Photographer RJ Sangosti has traveled the

country documenting the 2008 presidential

primaries and campaigns. He has been a Post photographer for four years.

Editor Kevin Dale is an assistant managing editor of The Denver Post. He is supervising the paper's coverage of the Democratic National Convention and presidential politics.

Designer Jeff Neumann is lead designer for The Denver Post. He came from The Seattle Times to work on the redesign of the Post about five years ago.

The Denver Post


August 24, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


Biden adds experience, but duo may clash


BYLINE: Chuck Plunkett The Denver Post


SECTION: Pg. P-03


LENGTH: 352 words


Barack Obama moved further toward the middle in his selection of Joe Biden by picking a longtime senator meant to prop up the relative newcomer on experience, experts said Saturday.

"(Obama) has been running on forging a new kind of politics," said Robert Eisinger, a political scientist at Lewis & Clark College in Portland, Ore. "By putting someone seasoned on the ticket, the message will be amended. It's now change with experience."

Biden, a Delaware senator since 1972, chairs the Foreign Relations Committee and is considered one of the top Washington insiders on international affairs. Obama, the presumptive Democratic nominee for president, is serving his first term in the Senate.

But the two men, so different in their campaign styles, might not mesh, Eisinger warned. If the Obama campaign, which expects tight discipline and keeps to script, tries to rein in the freewheeling Biden, it risks losing some of the magic.

"If he plays the disciplined Joe Biden, that's not Joe Biden at his best," Eisinger said, calling Biden's acceptance speech "uninspiring."

House Speaker Nancy Pelosi dismissed the idea that a Biden pick blunted Obama's promise to deliver change.

"Joe Biden has been a fighter for change," Pelosi said. "He has resisted the status quo.

"The Republicans want to have it both ways. They want to advertise that John McCain is the candidate of experience, and now they're saying experience in Washington, D.C., is not a good thing."

U.S. Sen. Ken Salazar said Biden would bring "tremendous energy" to the ticket.

"He is by far one of the best experts on foreign policy in the entire United States Senate and the entire country," Salazar said. "We need to put the world back together again, and Joe Biden is that person to complement Barack Obama's experience."

Mayor John Hickenlooper said the Delaware Democrat has been a leader in dealing with urban issues.

"He has consistently shown you don't throw money at problems," Hickenlooper said of Biden. "You come up with solutions."

Anne C. Mulkern and Allison Sherry contributed to this report.

Chuck Plunkett: 303-954-1333 or cplunkett@denverpost.com

McCain ad bemoans Clinton-less ticket


SECTION: Pg. P-03


LENGTH: 116 words


The campaign for John McCain expects to launch a new television ad today meant to fan flames of discontent among supporters of Hillary Rodham Clinton.

According to sources inside the McCain campaign, the ad, called "Passed Over," will air in Colorado and other battleground states. Using clips of Clinton during the primary race, the ad suggests that Barack Obama didn't pick Clinton as his running mate because she criticized his lack of experience and his association with convicted land developer Tony Rezko.

``She won millions of votes but isn't on the ticket,'' the ad says. ``Why? For speaking the truth on his plans. ... The truth hurt, and Obama didn't like it.'' Chuck Plunkett, The Denver Post

The Denver Post


August 24, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


GOP cuts funding for races Campaigns like Schaffer's will get less support because of fundraising woes.


BYLINE: Michael Riley The Denver Post


SECTION: DENVER & THE WEST; Pg. B-04


LENGTH: 356 words


Republicans hoping to capture the state's open Senate seat were dealt a setback Friday by national party leaders, who announced that their fundraising efforts were falling far behind expectations and immediately scaled back the money they plan to spend in Colorado.

The news could shift the dynamic of the state's Senate race, one in which both national parties were expected to spend heavily to support the prospects of their candidates, Republican Bob Schaffer and Democrat Mark Udall.

Last week, the national GOP's independent expenditure committee canceled about a quarter of the ad buys it had already booked in Colorado for this fall, according to public records, reducing the total from $4.8 million to $3.6 million.

The Republican Senate committee reported $25 million in the bank for July, compared with $42 million for the Democrats. But the Republicans also have more Senate seats to defend and more incumbents in closely contested races. Democrats have booked $4.2 million worth of TV time in Colorado between September and Nov. 4.

The bad news for Republicans was delivered by Nevada U.S. Sen. John Ensign - who heads the party's national Senate election effort - in an unusually frank statement issued Friday.

"I recently challenged my colleagues to step up to the plate and help me provide the resources our candidates need to compete in races across the country. ... It has become clear that my call has gone largely unanswered," Ensign said.

He went on to say he had no choice but to cut the money the national party could spend on individual Senate campaigns such as the one in Colorado.

Ensign's letter is in part a game of chicken, hoping to pry some of that money loose, Republicans say, but it is also an admission of just how poor a fundraising year 2008 has been for his party, affecting races on just about every level.

Last month, presumed Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama raised $51 million, compared with Republican John McCain's $27 million. And in Colorado, Udall had about $1 million more in the bank than Schaffer at the end of the last reporting period.

GOP slashes funding for Senate races As fundraising efforts lag, national party leaders plan to cut spending on individual campaigns, like Schaffer's.


BYLINE: Michael Riley The Denver Post


SECTION: DENVER & THE WEST; Pg. B-01


LENGTH: 398 words


Republicans hoping to capture the state's open Senate seat were dealt a setback Friday by national party leaders, who announced that their fundraising efforts were falling far behind expectations and immediately scaled back the money they plan to spend in Colorado.

The news could shift the dynamic of the state's hotly contested Senate race, one in which both national parties were expected to spend heavily to defend the prospects of their candidates, Republican Bob Schaffer and Democrat Mark Udall.

This week, the national GOP's independent expenditure committee canceled about a quarter of the ad buys it had already booked in Colorado for this fall, according to public records, reducing the total from $4.8 million to $3.6 million.

The Republican Senate committee reported $25 million in the bank for July, compared with $42 million for the Democrats. But the Republicans also have more Senate seats to defend and more incumbents in closely contested races. Democrats have booked $4.2 million worth of TV time in Colorado between September and Nov. 4 - Election Day.

The bad news for Republicans was delivered by Nevada U.S. Sen. John Ensign - who heads the party's national Senate election effort - in an unusually frank statement issued Friday.

"I recently challenged my colleagues to step up to the plate and help me provide the resources our candidates need to compete in races across the country ... It has become clear that my call has gone largely unanswered," Ensign said. He went on to say he had no choice but to cut the money the national party could spend on individual Senate campaigns such as the one in Colorado.

Much of the money for the National Republican Senatorial Committee comes from transfers from the campaign war chests of sitting senators, but those funds have simply not kept pace with those of their Democratic counterparts.

Ensign's letter is in part a game of chicken, hoping to prod some of that money loose, Republicans say, but it is also an admission of just how poor a fundraising year 2008 has been for his party, affecting races on just about every level.

Last month, presumed Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama raised $51 million compared to Republican John McCain's $27 million. And in Colorado, Udall had about $1 million more in the bank than Schaffer at the end of the last reporting period.

Presidential elections

Ads play to

 Obama's strengths

I can't resist responding to the John McCain commercials that recognize Barack Obama as a world celebrity but call into question his "readiness" to lead. While many political ads are notoriously slanted, this particular one plays into the hands of Obama's strengths. He is indeed seen worldwide as a celebrity. Leaders, people and countries around the world have recognized him as a leader and hailed him as their hope for the United States.

I have the honor to work with governmental and political leaders around the world in democratization assistance. In that capacity I have worked with them in over 30 countries. I think Americans would be surprised to see how much these leaders know and understand about our country and our political processes.

They follow closely what is happening in the United States and are constantly asking about the election and who will be the winner in the presidential contest. After viewing American actions for the past seven and a half years, they still look to America to be a strong leader, not one who is dictatorial and advocates preventive strikes and unilateral imperial overreach, but rather an America who engages in productive dialogue and respect for the art of negotiation first.

They look for a leader who will provide them hope that a change in current policies will occur. They look to see that once again traditional American values will be promoted where everyone can envision opportunities, not just the privileged few, and that countries can look to America for assistance, and at least, a modicum of respect for all.

So contrary to what Mr. McCain attempts to portray, the person he sees as being recognized as a leader and a celebrity is already here. He is on stage, and the rest of the world is anxiously awaiting his election and a change in the failed policies that Mr. McCain continues to support. Perhaps Mr. McCain would be better served not to recognize Mr. Obama's strengths and the world's recognition of those.

SHERRIE WOLFF

Boulder

Congress

Approval ratings

in the dump

T he magic numbers are nine and 14. Actually, it's 9 percent and 14 percent. These are the numbers of Americans who approve of the U. S. Congress in a recent Rasmussen poll and Gallup poll respectively. That means that a large percentage of Americans disapprove of the way that the U. S. Congress is conducting our business.

These are much lower than the approval rating of the president, which is around 30 percent. This raises some interesting questions for those who are paying attention to what is happening in our country.

If these numbers are so low, why are political pundits and analysts predicting that the Democrats will increase their number in both houses this Fall? Won't that mean that the ratings will go lower?

However, would they increase at all if the other party wins a majority? Recent history shows that the numbers, 9 percent and 14 percent, would probably not go up very much, if at all. But maybe it wouldn't decrease any more. This is just some food for thought for citizens who can still think for themselves and have not been brainwashed by politicians and the media.

Nancy Pelosi recently said that George Bush is a complete failure as president. Isn't that the "pot calling the skillet black?" This session of Congress is the most inept one in the history of America! All Americans should be concerned about the future of our great country with these two parties in control.

BOB PERDUE

Longmont

Elimination

a travesty

I found the fact that the Boulder City Council voted to eliminate the Spring Cleanup a travesty. Why are they doing it? They are saving the $300,000 cost for something of a higher priority.

What is the higher priority? It will "spend the program's $300,000 budget to help businesses reduce how much they put into landfills." The city obviously feels the Spring Cleanup is a worthless program that has nothing to do with keeping Boulder beautiful.

GEORGE NARCAVAGE

Boulder

Olympic coverage

NBC coverage

is unethical

L ike most of you track and field aficionados out there, I am deeply saddened and in dismay about the lack of integrity NBC has decided to display while broadcasting the games of the 29th Olympiad.

For instance, I set my alarm for 8:25 on the morning of Aug. 16 in hopes to witness a much anticipated 100m final. Instead I was confronted with a string of cheap, paid programming.

Half confused and half pissed off, I got on the Internet only to find that the race had already been run and NBC refused to broadcast it live, in hopes of better ratings if broadcasted at night. This was only the first of many disappointments. ... I had also stumbled upon numerous headlines with the bold upper

cased letters WR, obviously standing for world record, causing an uber-spoiler.

I received e-mails from my parents and friends in the Netherlands wanting to talk about this amazing race, which was broadcasted live on Eurosports, and had yet to be released in the United States. The only reason they don't show it live is too build up drama and to keep you in suspense about the actual start of the race, such that you waste an entire evening, glued to their contemptible programming in hopes to catch a glimpse of a 10-second race (after several commercials and build-ups of course).

This whole incident was just a small dose of unethical journalism. In the woman's 10,000m final (broadcasted eight hours after it was live), the winner was an Ethiopian lady by the name of Tirunesh Dibaba, followed by Abeylegesse, of Turkey, then Flanagan, of the USA. As impressive as a bronze medal is, the Camera was only focused on Flanagan and didn't even show Dibaba, or her flag, or her emotions for fulfilling her hard fought ambitions.

Honestly ... the woman trains five hours a day, give her some spotlight. This is classic American xenophobia at its finest.

DAVID ARMENTROUT

Boulder

Does Open Space and Mountain Parks offer night hikes for men only? Why is it necessary to make this type of activity exclusive to one sex?

Obama's DNC speech bad for working man

Sen. Barack Obama: man of the people, champion of the working man. How about some no-noise games at Coors Field? Re: "Why do Rockies games have to be so loud?" Aug. 16 letter to the editor.

Michael Mills, Lakewood

In 2nd CD primary, the election process worked

People who cite money as the only reason for Jared Polis' victory are underestimating the importance of the candidates' messages and the ability of the electorate to decide for themselves.

Every campaign begins with a message and a plan to explain that message to the voters. Jared preached change in the political realm and used his money to explain that message to the voters. Joan Fitz-Gerald ran on the status quo platform: that things are great for the Democrats in Colorado, and she can do the same things in Washington. She was able to get her message out with less money than Jared by utilizing lobbyists and political action committees, which are the traditional tools of the Colorado Democratic machinery.

How can Polis relate to middle class? Empathy

Re: "How can Polis relate to struggling middle class?" Aug. 19 letter to the editor.

Letter-writer Marie Cline expresses disbelief that Jared Polis could relate to the concerns of lower- and middle-class voters because of his millionaire status. McCain's response to criticism of his comments on the Colorado River The Colorado River Compact and water supplies in the Southwest are of great importance to me. My recent remarks may have been mistakenly construed as a call to rescind the compact and commence negotiations for new water allocations. Let me be clear that I do not advocate renegotiation of the compact. This is an approach that is forward-looking, and ensures cooperation in achieving implementation of the compact and the 2007 agreement among the states and the Department of Interior and is mindful of potential technological developments that could potentially reduce water demands in certain areas.

A McCain administration would mean an end to the federal command-and-control approach that has failed those of the West. As a Westerner, I fully understand that water is a precious commodity. A federally driven scheme to reopen the compact would run afoul of my longheld respect for the importance of state law and local prerogatives in the allocation of water resources. I understand the complexities of water policy, and under no circumstances would I move forward with Colorado River policies not supported by all the states involved.

Instead, the federal government can best assist the Colorado River Basin states by ensuring the cooperation of the Bureau of Reclamation in making water storage efficient and effective, in assisting with additional storage projects in both the upper and lower basins, and supporting states in implementing the compact. The states did a tremendous job in reaching agreement in 2007, and the federal government should simply assist them in furthering that agreement.

These are the principles that inform my approach to water policy, and I appreciate the opportunity to clarify them. I look forward to our continued productive dialogues on these issues as we work to find genuine solutions to the water challenges of the West.

U.S. Sen. John McCain,

Washington, D.C.

The Denver Post


August 21, 2008 Thursday
FINAL EDITION


Obama staying the course Despite polls that show him slipping, he's not changing his strategy


BYLINE: Karen E. Crummy The Denver Post


SECTION: A SECTION; Pg. A-06


LENGTH: 534 words


LYNCHBURG, VA. - Barack Obama's campaign Wednesday said that despite recent polls showing he and John McCain are in a statistical dead heat, the Democratic presidential candidate isn't making any changes and is sticking to his current strategy.

McCain, who has launched a number of TV ads over the past few weeks portraying Obama as inexperienced, liberal and a cult of personality, has steadily narrowed the gap between himself and Obama, according to polls released this week.

While McCain is solidifying his base, Obama faces challenges and is shedding voters he held a month ago as he heads into next week's Democratic National Convention.

Some of that success may be chalked up to McCain's attacks, but also his sharper focus on supporting offshore drilling - a position he only recently adopted.

Regardless, the Obama campaign appears less than panicked and is going forward with what it says has worked in the past, as well as relatively new, sharp attacks on McCain.

"If we ran the campaign based on polls, we would've dropped out a year ago," said spokeswoman Jen Psaki, who was traveling with the candidate. "We will continue to introduce him across the country and make the tough contrasts with John McCain."

Those contrasts started in earnest in the past few days when Obama returned from a Hawaii vacation. The timing is no accident, his campaign said, because as summer ends, people are starting to pay attention to the race.

As a result, Obama released a 30-second ad in Colorado and a number of other battleground states Wednesday that touts his plan to cut taxes for the middle class and contends John McCain's plan gives tax breaks to the oil industry and other corporations. On the campaign trail, Obama continues to tie McCain with the "failed policies" of President Bush.

Additionally, many political observers have noted that Obama is likely to get a bump after he announces his running mate, expected this weekend, and again after the convention.

But he goes into crunch time with some challenges.

Surveys indicate that the public still questions Obama's patriotism and whether he has enough experience to be president.

A recent Pew Research Center survey showed Obama leading McCain by 3 percentage points. Two months ago, Obama led by 8 points.

And a Reuters/Zogby poll released Wednesday showed Obama trailing McCain by 5 percentage points - a "dramatic reversal" from the same survey conducted last month showing Obama up by 7 percentage points, according to John Zogby. Obama lost support across the board - from Catholics, younger voters, women and independents.

"Conservatives were supposed to be the bigger problem for McCain," Zogby said. "Obama still has work to do on his base. At this point McCain seems to be doing a better job with his."

McCain is also not sitting idly by as he is attacked. He tried to portray Obama as "testy" Wednesday after Obama said the Arizona senator was questioning his patriotism.

And a new McCain radio ad slaps Obama as a tax-and-spend liberal.

"Celebrities like to spend their millions," the ad says. "Barack Obama is no different. Only it's your money he wants to spend."

Reuters contributed to this report.

Karen Crummy: 303-954-1594

or kcrummy@denverpost.com

The Denver Post


August 19, 2008 Tuesday
FINAL EDITION


An election just like any other


BYLINE: DAVID hARSANYI Denver Post Columnist


SECTION: Pg. B-11


LENGTH: 629 words


There is a palpable unease within the Democratic Party. After all, why hasn't Barack Obama pulled away from John McCain in the polls?

Here we are with a struggling economy, an unpopular war, high gas prices, mortgage meltdown and an old coot with a wicked temper running for the Republicans. Shouldn't the urbane and unflappable Obama be ahead by at least 14 points? What is wrong with Americans?

Obama himself has questioned the wisdom of voters, wondering at a gathering in San Francisco why, with all our tribulations, voters do not cry, "Toss the bums out, we're starting from scratch, we're starting over."

If only it were so simple.

It's often said that loathing of an opposing candidate is not enough for victory. And aversion to George Bush is not, on its own, enough reason to spur a realignment of the electorate. This election, in fact, despite the fruitless attempts of Obama, is a traditional battle between the left and the right. It's about policy and, the worst distraction of all, politics.

Initially, Obama seemed unwilling to participate in this repugnant world. On the other side, the more aggressively political McCain has become, the more Republicans seem to believe they can actually win this thing.

Candidates, of course, should not take elections too personally. Any two generic nominees are likely to split the nation somewhere in half. Our fundamental attitudes about the world do not change simply because we're stuck with an incompetent president, a bad spot in the economy or a spiffy ad.

At some point, though, we need to hear where candidates stand on policy. This is where Obama struggles. Politics is about divisions.

The first sign of trouble was energy. As soon as gas prices approached $4 a gallon, Republicans, sensing real anger, demanded more domestic energy production. Democrats found themselves in a dangerous spot; as sentinels of Earth's well-being, they were unwilling to cede that more drilling should even be part of a solution, calling it a "gimmick" and "hoax."

Well, now nearly every Democrat, including Obama, supports this "hoax." It's the sort of convenient policy shift that reminds us of ... well, of every politician who has ever walked the planet.

Such political calculations have been the trademark of Obama. On public finance for his campaign, immigration policy, international trade and retroactive immunity for phone companies, it's been about political expediency.

Obama recently told evangelical preacher Rick Warren that the most difficult decision he's ever made was his opposition to the Iraq (while in the Illinois legislature). Yet, even with his defining issue, Obama has modified his position as polls dictate.

So when we throw the bums out for the sake of change, what will we be changing to? What kind of policies would take the place of the ones wrought by those bums?

Politics as usual.

Americans, not long ago, rightly threw Republican bums out of Congress only to have them replaced by Nancy Pelosi and Harry Reid, two of the most ineffective political leaders in the memory of the republic.

Polls show that Congress now holds a lower approval rating (in the single digits) than the famously low approval rating of the president.

McCain, let's not forget, faces an enormous uphill battle, lacking money, excitement and support. He's certainly flipped and flopped with the best of 'em.

But in the end, this country is divided between right and left. Higher taxes or lower. Pro-choice or pro-life. Stay in Iraq or leave. Whatever your issue, there is no "we" in compromise. Why should there be? It's not about candidates, it's about policy.

And this election, despite the grandiose talk, is still about a narrowly divided nation.

It always has been.

Reach columnist David Harsanyi at 303-954-1255 or dharsanyi@

denverpost.com.

August 17, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


What's so funny about political humor? Sifting out the propaganda, comedians are shaping the dialogue in this election


BYLINE: John Wenzel The Denver Post


SECTION: ARTS & ENTERTAINMENT; Pg. E-01


LENGTH: 1282 words


The video opens with a throng gathered on Washington's National Mall before flitting through a series of death-by-association comparisons: John McCain is older than the "Golden Girls," the Crypt-Keeper from "Tales From the Crypt," Larry King, Colonel Sanders and Yoda.

Just two weeks later, it's already one of modern politics' greatest hits: Paris Hilton appears, reclining by a pool in a leopard-print bathing suit and talking smack about McCain's age. She runs through an improbably coherent energy policy before closing with: "I'm Paris Hilton, and I approve this message because I think it's totally hot."

One would be excused for wondering if this is what our political dialogue has been reduced to. But the truth is, comedy's time-honored role in politics extends deeper than an online video.

A response to a McCain ad comparing Barack Obama to lighter-than-air celebrities Hilton and Britney Spears, the video has snared nearly 7 million views on funnyordie.com.

Besides the fact that it's just plain funny, the number of hits points to comedy's ability to filter the sewage of the political process. The unprecedented barrage of political messages in this particular election cycle makes laughter more useful than ever.

"As a satirist, I have great faith in the founders of this country who believed that power turns everybody to crap," said comedian Harry Shearer.

Best known for his voice work on "The Simpsons" and roles in mockumentaries such as "This Is Spinal Tap," Shearer recently released the CD "Songs of the Bushmen." In keeping with his long-standing practice of political humor, it skewers the Bush administration in a series of musical "tributes."

Shearer hopes to perform some of those songs in Denver during the Democratic National Convention next week, an event that will attract a phalanx of comedians eager to attack politics' double-speak.

"I would expect the level of theater at this one to be Shakespearean," Shearer said. "And I say that having been a volunteer at the 1960 DNC in Los Angeles, where I saw Kennedy's acceptance speech."

"House of opportunity"

Increasingly sophisticated campaigns have muddled our ability to separate truth from propaganda, but viewing the politics through humor allows us to see it for what it is, said Wende Curtis, owner of Denver's Comedy Works.

"Politics in general is absolute bedlam. It's a big house of opportunity for comedians," she said. "It doesn't matter which side it is, and it doesn't matter who's in office or who's got control of the House. There's always going to be these various characters out there."

The right to mock our leaders is built into the Bill of Rights' free speech provisions, which paved the way for political newspaper cartoons to offer early Americans a satisfyingly irreverent view of corrupt figures.

Mort Sahl, the 1950s-bred wisecracker with the V-neck sweater and newspaper tucked under his arm, has made a career of deriding - and working for - a handful of American presidents. The tradition continues today with fake news shows - Comedy Central's "The Daily Show" and "The Colbert Report." The wildly popular programs serve as legitimate news sources for a chunk of the population.

"Comedy has always played a role in politics with late- night talk shows and things like that, but it's more and more important with the rise of stand-up on Comedy Central, and especially Stephen Colbert and Jon Stewart," said Jimmy Tingle, a talk-show veteran who has appeared as a commentator on MSNBC and "60 Minutes II."

"Comedy is an entertaining form of commentary that augments the discussion, because with political satire you can embellish and exaggerate and get upset about things," he said. "It wouldn't be right to make things up, but you at least have the ability to use your imagination and creativity."

Tingle will appear with fellow political comedians Scott Blakeman, Will Durst and John Marshall at "Breaking Convention: A Liberal Dose of Political Humor" at the Bug Theatre on Aug. 24, 26 and 27. He said the current election and convention season is uncharted territory in terms of candidates.

"A lot of undercurrents in the country are coming to the surface," he said. "Whenever there's tension it's always a good time for comedy, but the comedy itself has to be insightful."

Part of the challenge has been providing insight on relatively nontraditional politicians Obama and McCain. Some critics have charged that Obama is joke-proof, while others simply recycle stale Bob Dole jokes to mock McCain.

"I don't think it's that they're staying away from the race factor with (Obama)," said Comedy Works' Curtis. "But it is true that he gets made fun of less. It's not just with comedians but perhaps the media in general."

Shearer noted that part of it is Obama's lack of experience on the national stage.

"His rhetorical competence hasn't been seen since Bobby Kennedy, so of course the joke files are sort of bare. But I don't think there's any more of a protective shield around him - especially if he gets elected," Shearer said. "When you start making decisions, you start making mistakes."

While most comedians and entertainers tend to lean liberal, some express cynicism at the political process in general.

Truth-telling tradition

"If anything happens that actually means anything, I'll be ecstatic," said comedian Lewis Black of the upcoming conventions. "It's preaching to the converted. The last one I was running around at, in Boston, I was like, 'What are they doing this for?' It's just a pep rally."

Black, who plays the Pikes Peak Center in Colorado Springs on Thursday and the Ellie Caulkins Opera House on Friday, said that comedy's truth-telling tradition usually serves the average person.

"It points out that there's utterly no attempt by either party to do what they're supposed to, which is govern," he said. "All they can do is tell us what the other side is saying, and how awful they are. That's not the job. The job is to listen to the other side and come up with something that works with both."

The coming of the Democratic National Convention will provide a wealth of political comedy, from the bipartisan ribbing of the Capitol Steps troupe at the Arvada Center for the Performing Arts on Saturday to Bovine Metropolis Theater-hosted shows "Planet Washington" and "On the Spot," which run Aug. 25-28.

But this isn't your parents' political comedy. Mark Russell-style "piano-and-punch- line" humor still thrives, but so does shock-value stuff like the Rolling Stone cartoon depicting McCain being tortured in a cage by exaggerated Asian stereotypes, or "Saturday Night Live" spoofs that lampoon the media's perceived free pass for Obama.

Comedy's effect on politics may be difficult to measure, but its impact is undeniable. Iconic spoofs of presidents from Gerald Ford to George W. Bush have been stock-in- trade at "Saturday Night Live" for three decades, and politicians themselves have often popped up on comedy shows: Witness McCain's frequent appearances on "The Daily Show" and "SNL." (McCain's daughter Meghan briefly worked at the latter program.)

Having a sense of humor about yourself clearly pays off - but only if the tone is right.

"If it's not good, it doesn't work," Lizz Winstead, co-creator of "The Daily Show," told The Denver Post in April. "As with anything, people go to see comedy for very different reasons, and if you're somebody who likes to see comedy to escape, then you probably won't go see a political comic anyway. But if you're somebody that pays attention to the world and likes catharsis, and laughing about irony of the world, and the way politicians work, then you'll miss it when it's not there."

John Wenzel: 303-954-1642 or jwenzel@denverpost.com

The Denver Post


August 17, 2008 Sunday
FINAL EDITION


Flip-flop or reason?


BYLINE: Ed Quillen Denver Post Columnist


SECTION: PERSPECTIVE; Pg. D-04


LENGTH: 593 words


Since this is an election season, we will hear plenty about flip-flops. If a candidate changes from a position he supports to a position he opposes, then it's a flip-flop, perhaps even a betrayal. If the candidate changes the other way, then it must represent enlightenment, an ability to learn from experience and adapt to changing circumstances.

For instance, the Sen. John McCain who supported the invasion of Iraq in 2003 has evolved into the John McCain who said last week that "in the 21st century, nations don't invade other nations." This clearly demonstrates his maturation as a statesman.

You want a flip-flop? There's the Foreign Intelligence Surveillance Act, which effectively granted immunity to telecommunication companies that helped spy on Americans without a warrant.

Last fall, Sen. Barack Obama said he would support a filibuster against renewing FISA if it included an immunity provision. Then there was the Obama who last month voted for just such a bill. That was a flip-flop because he moved from a position I supported - i.e., that the Fourth Amendment means something - to one I oppose. Other people, though, might see it as a nececessary adjustment in a political system based on compromise.

I liked the John McCain who could opposed torture with clear moral authority. Waterboarding "is a horrible torture technique," he said, and "People who have worn the uniform and had the experience know that this is a terrible and odious practice and should never be condoned in the U.S. We are a better nation than that."

But we are not a better nation because leaders like McCain, who could make us one, don't. McCain ended up voting against a bill that would have banned waterboarding by American intelligence agencies. Clearly a flip-flop, or maybe even a betrayal.

I felt much the same way about Rep. Mark Udall. Not because he just announced he'd support some offshore drilling after opposing it, but because he, like Obama, voted for the FISA bill. Udall had voted against the Patriot Act. I figured that if he had the backbone to do that, during the clamor of late 2001, then he actually cared about our rights and liberties.

Others might see this not as a flip-flop, but as recognition that there's a difference between representing one congressional district as opposed to an entire state in the U.S. Senate.

As for his opponent, Republican Bob Schaffer, I thought it was admirable that he promised to serve no more than three terms in the House of Representatives and that he actually kept that promise, unlike Tom Tancredo and Scott McInnis.Then again, we ended up with Marilyn Musgrave in his old seat.

Anyway, it's weird to see a commercial where Schaffer stands in front of windmills after he voted against incentives for alternate energy sources in 2001. Is he flip-flopping or recognizing new realities?

I'm glad to see that Schaffer opposes Amendment 48, the Colorado ballot initiative that would grant full constitutional protection to zygotes. But how does that opposition square with his 2000 statement that "From the moment of conception, this being is alive."

When you get right down to it, flip-flopping is our fault. We expect politicians to have steadfast principles, and we also expect them to respond to public opinion. Some do stand by their principles in the face of public opinion. We vote them out of office, and then wait about 65 years to honor them. Just ask Ralph Carr.

Ed Quillen (ed@cozine.com) is a freelance writer, history buff, publisher of Colorado Central Magazine in Salida and frequent contributor to The Post.

Daily Camera (Boulder, Colorado)


August 15, 2008 Friday
First Edition


SECTION: A:EDITORIAL; Pg. 10


LENGTH: 1016 words


Democratic convention

History

repeated?

T here is the old phrase that the more things change, the more they stay the same. Consider the following:

The year is '08. There is a second-term Republican in the White House who is not going to run again. The Democrats decide to hold their national convention in Denver. One reason for selecting Denver is to show that they believe the West is becoming an increasingly important part of the country. They hope their candidate will raise the bar. In fact, one of his names not only contains the letters "b-a-r" but actually starts with a B. He has a strong Illinois connection and is known as a good orator.

The above is not only true for 2008, but also for 1908! (In 1908, Teddy Roosevelt was the second-term Republican, and William Jennings Bryan the Democratic candidate. Bryan was born and raised in Illinois and was considered a good speaker including his famous "Cross of Gold" speech). However, in at least one respect, the Democrats hope it will be different this time around: In 1908 they lost...

STEVE LEVIN

Boulder

Jared Polis

'Focus on the man,

not the money'

M oney, money, money. Even a pat on the back from the Camera comes out as "money." Money put my brother in contention, sure. But what about the fact that he is exciting, brilliant, generous, young and infectiously energetic about bringing change to Washington?

What about the fact that in 2000, at the age of 25, he helped to launch the blue wave by becoming only the second state-wide elected Democrat in Colorado after then-Attorney General Ken Salazar (now there are 4, going on 5)? Rarely mentioned, too, is the rather relevant fact that Jared was the only of the three candidates who advocates and indeed has crusaded for serious campaign finance reform and ethics laws at state and federal levels. I don't expect the Camera to be adulatory, but for heaven's sake: Focus on the man, not the money -- the electorate did!

JORIAN POLIS SCHUTZ

Boulder

Downtown Lafayette

Move could

jump-start economy

I n response to the Open Forum letter (Camera, Aug. 14) titled "Lafayette move helps downtown." I could not agree more with Susan Churchill's view of the situation. I have been a Lafayette resident for 15 years. As a supporter of keeping

commerce local, I urge all Lafayette citizens to get behind this movement to relocate the proposed site for Boulder County Housing Authority's housing project.

The downtown business district has always suffered from business turnover and large percentage of office vacancy rates. I can't tell you how many empty offices occupy the downtown area, the numbers are staggering. With the exit of Wal-Mart and Albertsons, downtown Lafayette has lost its largest retail anchor. In its wake, is urban blight in the form of a large empty shell of a strip mall.

We need people and shoppers living and working and shopping in downtown Lafayette. I can't think of a better way to support urban renewal than relocate the Boulder County Housing Authority project to the old Albertson shopping center area. The proximity to shopping, public transportation and other activities are right there. The infrastructure is in place and ready to develop.

Why put people in need of city services out away from downtown when that is exactly where the city services are located. Having people live, work and recreate in downtown Lafayette is what the town needs to jump-start it economic survival. Please support our town and urge the County Commissioners to move the Boulder County Housing Authority proposed site to downtown Lafayette.

MARK FORD

Lafayette

Presidential elections

Understanding the

world's humanity

A s a former member of the U.S. National Shotgun team, I've been firmly embedded in the conservative culture for more than 12 years now. Through my experiences, I've learned how much can be accomplished by one person, and how much more can be accomplished by a group of hard-working people banding together.

But more importantly, I've also learned that our first loyalties are to the values and principles that illuminate the moral fabric of the American people. Certainly, we can point to the traditional American values of freedom, equality, and respect for human life that have repeatedly lead to progress within our nation and to our leadership in the international community.

I've also come to notice that this presidential campaign is showcasing one of the greatest exemplars of the great American tradition that we've seen in quite a while. Barack Obama represents this nation's tradition of inspiring cooperation and tolerance -- of protecting our personal

freedoms -- and of respecting human life, wherever it may be found.

As I traveled to competitions around the world, I learned firsthand that people in America, and in all the countries of the world, are all real people -- with lives and friends and families. Whereas McCain selfishly claims America has a writ to prosperity over other nations, Barack Obama understands the world's common humanity and has taken the traditional American values to the world stage -- advocating freedom, equality, and respect for human life both in America and abroad.

KEN VAIL

Colorado Springs

Shield laws

Defending the

First Amendment

R e: "Stutzman: FBI apology shows need ... c" As a longtime newsperson, I'm surprised to see so many in the press who have so little faith in the Constitution that they believe they need a federal statute to prop up their secret activities. I think it's a bad idea, for whatever privilege Congress bestows, it can license, regulate, amend, tweak or use as a weapon. Some politicians have even been known to expect return favors.

The press already has its own special shield law: the First Amendment. That we in the press have done such a lousy job in not only defending it vigorously but in educating the judiciary and the public about its unique meaning, is our own fault. Now we want the politicians to bail us out with a device that we take to them with hat in hand, and already there is partisan bickering. "Trust me, said the spider to the fly."

PERCY CONARROE

Longmont

Daily Camera (Boulder, Colorado)


August 10, 2008 Sunday
First Edition


Don't tick off the blonde


SECTION: B:DAILY CAMERA


LENGTH: 547 words


T his week we were reminded not to pick on the blonde. She might, like sleeping beauty, wake to realize she has something to say and, lookie here, she can say it to a bank of cameras.

We first learned this from Princess Diana who knew she could get cameras to follow her anywhere, and she led them to the land mines of Africa, revealing the ravaged lives of innocent civilians. Her efforts resulted, after her death, in the signing of the Ottowa Treaty to ban landmines.

Early on, Diana was widely seen an imbecile, but by the end with her brilliant use of publicity she spanked the House of Windsor.

Paris Hilton may be smartening up likewise with a comic video launched this week at the expense of John McCain whose recent ad tried to insult Barack Obama by lumping him with celebrities like herself and Britney Spears. And lo, Paris the impish heiress pushed back, showing McCain as "the oldest celebrity alive," replete with rotting face. Then, to everyone's amazement, she dissected energy policy.

Many thought Paris did a fine job of discussing policy. Actually, she got the sequence backwards. Her scheme foresaw hyper-efficient vehicles coming to our rescue after

new oil might flow from new, limited offshore drilling. In reality hybrid and electric car technologies are on the rise right now, and we can't wait for new oil to arrive as a pretext to step up our efficient technologies as well as our mass transit.

If we can get new oil to flow in reasonably safe ways from domestic sources -- great, bring it on -- but it better go into cars that get over 100 miles per gallon. Our use of oil from any source should be as cleverly efficient as Paris's peekaboo swimsuit.

Stretched like linguini upon a plastic chaise lounge to discuss energy policy, Paris is living up to my long-held view of her potential. She should use her campy comedic ways as well as her platinum card contacts to speak out about energy and climate for the sake of her family's fortune which is tied up in beachfront properties around the world. A room at the Hilton would

be hard to enjoy at several hundred dollars per night when high tide is lapping around the legs of the concierge desk.

If Paris wants to get back in her granddaddy Barron Hilton's graces (he disinherited her last year for her juvenile antics), a fruitful path might be by inventing a joint effort between the hotel industry and the National Center for Atmospheric Research in Boulder (note to Paris: it's also headed up by a man named Barron). For the lack of a measly half-a-million dollars per year in funding, NCAR just shut down a program that focuses on strengthening poor countries' abilities to forecast and withstand droughts and floods stemming from climate change. Yet, with 630 hotels in the spectacular settings most threatened by climate change -- from Quito and Cameroon to Algeria and Abu Dhabi -- Hilton Hotels is uniquely positioned to need climate change forecasting and help its immediate and very poor neighbors as it helps itself.

In lobbying for such a cause Paris could fly around the world in all manner of skimpy and glorious attire and get it photographed for Vogue. A girl can't rely just on comedy Web sites to make a difference in the world. But using any of these tools is nothing Princess Diana wouldn't do.

The Denver Post


August 8, 2008 Friday
FINAL EDITION


Television by Joanne Ostrow Get your ringside seats ready for Games' high-definition spectacle


SECTION: Pg. D-01


LENGTH: 620 words


NBC's hype machine

is in high gear for

tonight's start of the 2008 Summer

Olympics. Before the torch is lit, viewers may need help sorting the advertising claims from the truth.

The opening ceremonies begin tonight at 6:30 locally on KUSA- Channel 9. After seeing two dress rehearsals, NBC this week expanded its planned coverage to 4 1/2 hours, 6:30-11 p.m., with assurances that this will be an "over the top" spectacle.

If you're in for the whole marathon, stay hydrated.

Between tonight's pomp and the closing ceremonies' circumstance on Aug. 24, expect much streaming video, cross-promotion of NBC's sister networks and Nike logos (they're a sponsor).

Aiming to avoid criticism it encountered in past years about tape delays, NBC will offer some 2,900 hours live, despite the time difference from China to the U.S. The broadcaster flexed some muscle with the International Olympic Committee and ordered the most popular sports moved to morning times in Beijing, to be live for prime time in most of the U.S.

Answers to a few FAQs:

Q: Will I be able to see everything in HD?

A: NBC is trumpeting the record amount of high-definition coverage for its 3,600 hours of coverage from Beijing. That's true as far as it goes. But you'll have to get CNBC HD, USA HD and Universal HD (available by subscription from Comcast and satellite providers DirecTV and Dish Network) in order to see many of the sports in high-def. The Universal HD feed is a simulcast of MSNBC.

Coverage on MSNBC, Telemundo, Oxygen, Bravo and the regular CNBC and USA networks will not be in HD.

Q: NBC has advertised the fact that it will have specialized soccer and basketball channels covering every single game in those sports. Where can I find them?

A: Not on Comcast, despite earlier assurances to the contrary. Try satellite.

The fact is, unless you have DirecTV or Dish services in Colorado, you're out of luck. NBC claims Comcast has reneged on a deal to carry the special sports networks. Comcast says each system is free to decide and that "we try to offer something for everyone."

(They're not offering the Olympic Chinese or Korean language channels, either.)

Q: What about HD on NBC?

A: Yes, you'll see the network's coverage in HD, but you won't get the full benefit of the technology without the fiber optics provided by cable or satellite delivery and, on your end, an HD-ready television set. If you're planning to use the rabbit ears on a vintage set, don't expect it to look like much.

Q: What's the advantage to video on demand?

A: Digital cable subscribers can get daily highlights at no extra charge. Pregame preview clips are available now. This could be a timesaver if you have specialized interests. Computer users may find live online streaming (at work!) of 25 sports even better.

Q: Will any commercials jump out?

A: This isn't the Super Bowl, when advertisers can reach a huge crowd in a condensed period. Spread over 17 days, the Olympiad is a different animal. Note, however, both presidential candidates have purchased network and cable ad time (John McCain's campaign spent $6 million, Barack Obama's spent $5 million, according to Advertising Age), so expect political pitches.

Q: What will NBC Universal consider success?

A: Other than piling up ad revenue, the goal is to exceed the 203 million U.S. viewers who sampled broadcasts from Athens in August 2004.

Q: What will make the broadcast a success?

A: Evenhanded commentary that avoids a xenophobic tilt; helpful yet nonintrusive graphics; adequate attention to protests and other news; and a steady flow of information about the host country that doesn't treat China as a theme park could make this the most exciting Olympics in years.

Joanne Ostrow: 303-954-1830 or jostrow@denverpost.com
